The Mayor of Paris, [Anne Hidalgo](/wiki/Anne_Hidalgo "Anne Hidalgo"), declared that this illustrated the "de-densification" of the city, creating more green space and less crowding.^[\[154\]](#cite_note-155)^^[\[155\]](#cite_note-156)^ Despite the drop, Paris remains the most densely-populated city in Europe, with 252 residents per hectare, not counting parks.^[\[156\]](#cite_note-Le_Monde,_22_January_2019-157)^ This drop was attributed partly to a lower birth rate, the departure of middle-class residents and the possible loss of housing in Paris, due to short-term rentals for tourism.^[\[157\]](#cite_note-ReferenceA-158)^{#cite_ref-ReferenceA_158-0}

Paris is the fourth largest municipality in the European Union, after [Berlin](/wiki/Berlin "Berlin"), [Madrid](/wiki/Madrid "Madrid") and [Rome](/wiki/Rome "Rome"). [Eurostat](/wiki/Eurostat "Eurostat") places Paris (6.5 million people) behind London (8 million) and ahead of Berlin (3.5 million), based on the 2012 populations of what Eurostat calls "urban audit core cities".^[\[158\]](#cite_note-159)^ The population of Paris today is lower than its historical peak of 2.9 million in 1921.^[\[159\]](#cite_note-160)^ The principal reasons are a significant decline in household size, and a dramatic migration of residents to the suburbs between 1962 and 1975. Factors in the migration included [de-industrialisation](/wiki/De-industrialisation "De-industrialisation"), high rent, the [gentrification](/wiki/Gentrification "Gentrification") of many inner quarters, the transformation of living space into offices, and greater affluence among working families. Paris's population loss came to a temporary halt at the beginning of the 21st century. The population increased from 2,125,246 in 1999 to 2,240,621 in 2012, before declining again slightly in 2017, 2018, and again in 2021.^[\[160\]](#cite_note-161)^^[\[161\]](#cite_note-pophis-162)^{#cite_ref-pophis_162-0}

Paris is the core of a built-up area that extends well beyond its limits: commonly referred to as the *agglomération Parisienne* , and statistically as a *[unité urbaine](/wiki/Unit%C3%A9_urbaine "Unité urbaine")* (a measure of [urban area](/wiki/Urban_area "Urban area")), the Paris agglomeration's population of 10,785,092 in 2017 made it the [largest urban area in the European Union](/wiki/List_of_urban_areas_in_the_European_Union "List of urban areas in the European Union").^[\[162\]](#cite_note-pop_UU-163)^^[\[163\]](#cite_note-urbaneurope-164)^ City-influenced commuter activity reaches further, in a statistical [*aire d'attraction* de Paris](/wiki/Paris_Metropolitan_Area "Paris Metropolitan Area"), "functional area", a statistical method comparable to a [metropolitan area](/wiki/Metropolitan_area "Metropolitan area"),^[\[164\]](#cite_note-FU-165)^), that had a population of 13,024,518 in 2017,^[\[165\]](#cite_note-pop_AU-166)^ 19.6% of the population of France,^[\[166\]](#cite_note-167)^ and the [largest metropolitan area](/wiki/List_of_metropolitan_areas_in_Europe "List of metropolitan areas in Europe") in the [Eurozone](/wiki/Eurozone "Eurozone").^[\[163\]](#cite_note-urbaneurope-164)^{#cite_ref-urbaneurope_164-1}

In 2012, according to [Eurostat](/wiki/Eurostat "Eurostat"), the EU statistical agency, in 2012 the Commune of Paris was the most densely populated city in the European Union. There were 21,616 people per square kilometre within the city limits, the NUTS-3 statistical area, ahead of Inner London West, which had 10,374 people per square kilometre. In the same census, three departments bordering Paris, [Hauts-de-Seine](/wiki/Hauts-de-Seine "Hauts-de-Seine"), [Seine-Saint-Denis](/wiki/Seine-Saint-Denis "Seine-Saint-Denis") and [Val-de-Marne](/wiki/Val-de-Marne "Val-de-Marne"), had population densities of over 10,000 people per square kilometre, ranking among the 10 most densely populated areas of the EU.^[\[167\]](#cite_note-168)^^\[*[verification needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability "Wikipedia:Verifiability")*\]^{#cite_ref-168}  

### Migration {#Migration}

Under French law, people born in foreign countries with no French citizenship at birth are defined as immigrants. In the 2012 census, 135,853 residents of the City of Paris were immigrants from [Europe](/wiki/Europe "Europe"), 112,369 were immigrants from the [Maghreb](/wiki/Maghreb "Maghreb"), 70,852 from [sub-Saharan Africa](/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa "Sub-Saharan Africa") and [Egypt](/wiki/Egypt "Egypt"), 5,059 from [Turkey](/wiki/Turkey "Turkey"), 91,297 from [Asia](/wiki/Asia "Asia") outside Turkey, 38,858 from the [Americas](/wiki/Americas "Americas"), and 1,365 from the [South Pacific](/wiki/Oceania "Oceania").^[\[168\]](#cite_note-169)^{#cite_ref-169}

In the Paris Region, 590,504 residents were immigrants from Europe, 627,078 were immigrants from the Maghreb, 435,339 from sub-Saharan Africa and Egypt, 69,338 from Turkey, 322,330 from Asia outside Turkey, 113,363 from the Americas, and 2,261 from the South Pacific.^[\[169\]](#cite_note-170)^{#cite_ref-170}

In 2012, there were 8,810 British citizens and 10,019 United States citizens living in the City of Paris (Ville de Paris), and 20,466 British citizens and 16,408 United States citizens living in the entire Paris Region ([Île-de-France](/wiki/%C3%8Ele-de-France "Île-de-France")).^[\[170\]](#cite_note-171)^^[\[171\]](#cite_note-172)^{#cite_ref-172}

In 2020--2021, about 6 million people, or 41% of the population of the Paris Region, were either immigrants (21%) or had at least one immigrant parent (20%). These figures do not include French people born in Overseas France and their direct descendants.^[\[172\]](#cite_note-173)^{#cite_ref-173}  

### Religion {#Religion}

See also: [Religious buildings in Paris](/wiki/Religious_buildings_in_Paris "Religious buildings in Paris")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Le_sacre_coeur.jpg/220px-Le_sacre_coeur.jpg)](/wiki/File:Le_sacre_coeur.jpg) [Sacré-Cœur](/wiki/Sacr%C3%A9-C%C5%93ur,_Paris "Sacré-Cœur, Paris") in [Montmartre](/wiki/Montmartre "Montmartre")

At the beginning of the twentieth century, Paris was the largest [Catholic](/wiki/Catholic_Church "Catholic Church") city in the world.^[\[173\]](#cite_note-174)^ French census data does not contain information about religious affiliation.^[\[174\]](#cite_note-175)^ In a 2011 survey by the [Institut français d'opinion publique](/wiki/Institut_fran%C3%A7ais_d%27opinion_publique "Institut français d'opinion publique") (IFOP), a French public opinion research organisation, 61 percent of residents of the Paris Region (Île-de-France) identified themselves as [Roman Catholic](/wiki/Catholic_Church "Catholic Church"). In the same survey, 7 percent of residents identified themselves as Muslims, 4 percent as Protestants, 2 percent as Jewish and 25 percent as without religion.{#cite_ref-175}

According to the INSEE, between 4 and 5 million French residents were born, or had at least one parent born, in a predominantly Muslim country, particularly [Algeria](/wiki/Algeria "Algeria"), [Morocco](/wiki/Morocco "Morocco") and [Tunisia](/wiki/Tunisia "Tunisia"). An IFOP survey in 2008 reported that, of immigrants from these predominantly Muslim countries, 25 percent went to the mosque regularly. 41 percent practised the religion, and 34 percent were believers, but did not practice the religion.^[\[175\]](#cite_note-176)^^[\[176\]](#cite_note-177)^ In 2012 and 2013, it was estimated that there were almost 500,000 Muslims in the City of Paris, 1.5 million Muslims in the Île-de-France region and 4 to 5 million Muslims in France.^[\[177\]](#cite_note-178)^^[\[178\]](#cite_note-179)^{#cite_ref-179}

In 2014, the Jewish population of the Paris Region was estimated to be 282,000, the largest concentration of Jews in the world outside of [Israel](/wiki/Israel "Israel") and the United States.^[\[179\]](#cite_note-180)^{#cite_ref-180}  

Economy {#Economy}
------------------

Main article: [Economy of Paris](/wiki/Economy_of_Paris "Economy of Paris")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/La_D%C3%A9fense_depuis_l%27Arc_de_Triomphe_Janvier_2023_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-La_D%C3%A9fense_depuis_l%27Arc_de_Triomphe_Janvier_2023_%28cropped%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:La_D%C3%A9fense_depuis_l%27Arc_de_Triomphe_Janvier_2023_(cropped).jpg) [La Défense](/wiki/La_D%C3%A9fense "La Défense"), the largest dedicated business district in Europe, located to the west of Paris^[\[180\]](#cite_note-France.fr-181)^ [![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Italiens12.jpg/220px-Italiens12.jpg)](/wiki/File:Italiens12.jpg) The [headquarters](/wiki/Headquarters "Headquarters") of [BNP Paribas](/wiki/BNP_Paribas "BNP Paribas"), the largest banking group in Europe, in the [Boulevard des Italiens](/wiki/Boulevard_des_Italiens "Boulevard des Italiens").^[\[181\]](#cite_note-182)^ [![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Hotel_de_la_vaupaliere54.jpg/220px-Hotel_de_la_vaupaliere54.jpg)](/wiki/File:Hotel_de_la_vaupaliere54.jpg) [Axa](/wiki/Axa "Axa") headquarters at Hôtel de La Vaupalière [![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Campus_Evergreen.jpg/220px-Campus_Evergreen.jpg)](/wiki/File:Campus_Evergreen.jpg) The [Crédit Agricole](/wiki/Cr%C3%A9dit_Agricole "Crédit Agricole") headquarters in [Montrouge](/wiki/Montrouge "Montrouge"), south of Paris^[\[182\]](#cite_note-sieges-183)^

The economy of the City of Paris is based largely on services and commerce. Of the 390,480 enterprises in Paris, 80.6 percent are engaged in commerce, transportation, and diverse services, 6.5 percent in construction, and 3.8 percent in industry.^[\[183\]](#cite_note-184)^ The story is similar in the [Paris Region](/wiki/Paris_Region "Paris Region") (Île-de-France): 76.7 percent of enterprises are engaged in commerce and services, and 3.4 percent in industry.^[\[184\]](#cite_note-185)^{#cite_ref-185}

At the 2012 census, 59.5% of jobs in the Paris Region were in market services (12.0% in wholesale and retail trade, 9.7% in professional, scientific, and technical services, 6.5% in information and communication, 6.5% in transportation and warehousing, 5.9% in finance and insurance, 5.8% in administrative and support services, 4.6% in accommodation and food services, and 8.5% in various other market services), 26.9% in non-market services (10.4% in human health and social work activities, 9.6% in public administration and defence, and 6.9% in education), 8.2% in manufacturing and utilities (6.6% in manufacturing and 1.5% in utilities), 5.2% in construction, and 0.2% in agriculture.^[\[185\]](#cite_note-186)^^[\[186\]](#cite_note-187)^{#cite_ref-187}

The Paris Region had 5.4 million salaried employees in 2010, of whom 2.2 million were concentrated in 39 *pôles d'emplois* or business districts. The largest of these, in terms of number of employees, is known in French as the QCA, or *quartier central des affaires* . In 2010, it was the workplace of 500,000 salaried employees, about 30 percent of the salaried employees in Paris and 10 percent of those in the Île-de-France. The largest sectors of activity in the central business district were finance and insurance (16 percent of employees in the district) and business services (15 percent). The district includes a large concentration of department stores, shopping areas, hotels and restaurants, as well a government offices and ministries.^[\[187\]](#cite_note-INSEE-188)^{#cite_ref-INSEE_188-0}

The second-largest business district in terms of employment is [La Défense](/wiki/La_D%C3%A9fense "La Défense"), just west of the city. In 2010, it was the workplace of 144,600 employees, of whom 38 percent worked in finance and insurance, 16 percent in business support services. Two other important districts, [Neuilly-sur-Seine](/wiki/Neuilly-sur-Seine "Neuilly-sur-Seine") and [Levallois-Perret](/wiki/Levallois-Perret "Levallois-Perret"), are extensions of the Paris business district and of La Défense. Another district, including [Boulogne-Billancourt](/wiki/Boulogne-Billancourt "Boulogne-Billancourt"), [Issy-les-Moulineaux](/wiki/Issy-les-Moulineaux "Issy-les-Moulineaux") and the southern part of the 15th arrondissement, is a centre of activity for the media and information technology.^[\[187\]](#cite_note-INSEE-188)^{#cite_ref-INSEE_188-1}

In 2021, the top French companies listed in the [Fortune Global 500](/wiki/Fortune_Global_500 "Fortune Global 500") all have their headquarters in the Paris Region. Six are in the central business district of the City of Paris, four are close to the city in the [Hauts-de-Seine](/wiki/Hauts-de-Seine "Hauts-de-Seine") Department, three are in [La Défense](/wiki/La_D%C3%A9fense "La Défense") and one is in [Boulogne-Billancourt](/wiki/Boulogne-Billancourt "Boulogne-Billancourt"). Some companies, like [Société Générale](/wiki/Soci%C3%A9t%C3%A9_G%C3%A9n%C3%A9rale "Société Générale"), have offices in both Paris and La Défense. The Paris Region is France's leading region for economic activity, with a [GDP](/wiki/Gross_domestic_product "Gross domestic product") of [€](/wiki/Euro "Euro")765 billion, of which €253 billion was in Paris city.^[\[188\]](#cite_note-189)^ In 2021, its GDP ranked first among the metropolitan regions of the EU, and its per-capita GDP PPP was the 8th highest.^[\[189\]](#cite_note-190)^^[\[190\]](#cite_note-191)^^[\[191\]](#cite_note-McKinsey-192)^ While the Paris region's population accounted for 18.8 percent of metropolitan France in 2019,^[\[3\]](#cite_note-pop2025-3)^ the Paris region's GDP accounted for 32 percent of metropolitan France's GDP.^[\[192\]](#cite_note-193)^^[\[193\]](#cite_note-194)^{#cite_ref-194}

The Paris Region economy has gradually shifted from industry to high-value-added service industries ([finance](/wiki/Financial_services "Financial services"), IT services) and high-tech manufacturing (electronics, optics, aerospace, etc.).^[\[194\]](#cite_note-INSEE_201210-195)^ The Paris region's most intense economic activity through the central [Hauts-de-Seine](/wiki/Hauts-de-Seine "Hauts-de-Seine") department and suburban La Défense business district places Paris's economic centre to the west of the city, in a triangle between the *[Opéra Garnier](/wiki/Palais_Garnier "Palais Garnier")*, *La Défense* and the *[Val de Seine](/wiki/Val_de_Seine "Val de Seine")* .^[\[194\]](#cite_note-INSEE_201210-195)^ While the Paris economy is dominated by [services](/wiki/Service_Sector "Service Sector"), and employment in manufacturing sector has declined sharply, the region remains an important manufacturing centre, particularly for aeronautics, automobiles, and "eco" industries.^[\[194\]](#cite_note-INSEE_201210-195)^{#cite_ref-INSEE_201210_195-2}

In the 2017 worldwide cost of living survey by the [Economist Intelligence Unit](/wiki/Economist_Intelligence_Unit "Economist Intelligence Unit"), based on a survey made in September 2016, Paris ranked as the seventh most expensive city in the world, and the second most expensive in Europe, after [Zürich](/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich "Zürich").^[\[195\]](#cite_note-196)^ In 2018, Paris was the most expensive city in the world with [Singapore](/wiki/Singapore "Singapore") and [Hong Kong](/wiki/Hong_Kong "Hong Kong").^[\[196\]](#cite_note-197)^ [Station F](/wiki/Station_F "Station F") is a [business incubator](/wiki/Business_incubator "Business incubator") for startups, noted as the world's largest startup facility.^[\[197\]](#cite_note-198)^{#cite_ref-198}  

### Employment and income {#Employment_and_income}

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Median_income_of_Paris_and_inner_ring_%282018%29_named.svg/220px-Median_income_of_Paris_and_inner_ring_%282018%29_named.svg.png)](/wiki/File:Median_income_of_Paris_and_inner_ring_(2018)_named.svg) The median income in Paris and its nearest departments, 2018. High income in red, low income in yellow

In 2007, the majority of Paris's salaried employees filled 370,000 businesses services jobs, concentrated in the north-western 8th, 16th and 17th arrondissements.^[\[198\]](#cite_note-AlapageINSEE-199)^ Paris's financial service companies are concentrated in the central-western 8th and 9th arrondissement banking and insurance district.^[\[198\]](#cite_note-AlapageINSEE-199)^ Paris's department store district in the 1st, 6th, 8th and 9th arrondissements employ ten percent of mostly female Paris workers, with 100,000 of these in the retail trade.^[\[198\]](#cite_note-AlapageINSEE-199)^ Fourteen percent of Parisians worked in hotels and restaurants and other services to individuals.^[\[198\]](#cite_note-AlapageINSEE-199)^{#cite_ref-AlapageINSEE_199-3}

Nineteen percent of Paris employees work for the State in either administration or education. The majority of Paris's healthcare and social workers work at the hospitals and social housing, concentrated in the peripheral 13th, 14th, 18th, 19th and 20th arrondissements.^[\[198\]](#cite_note-AlapageINSEE-199)^ Outside Paris, the western Hauts-de-Seine department [La Défense](/wiki/La_D%C3%A9fense "La Défense") district specialising in finance, insurance and scientific research district, employs 144,600.^[\[194\]](#cite_note-INSEE_201210-195)^ The north-eastern Seine-Saint-Denis audiovisual sector has 200 media firms and 10 major film studios.^[\[194\]](#cite_note-INSEE_201210-195)^{#cite_ref-INSEE_201210_195-4}

Paris's manufacturing is mostly focused in its suburbs. Paris has around 75,000 manufacturing workers, most of which are in the textile, clothing, leather goods, and shoe trades.^[\[194\]](#cite_note-INSEE_201210-195)^ In 2015, the Paris region's 800 aerospace companies employed 100,000.^[\[194\]](#cite_note-INSEE_201210-195)^ Four hundred automobile industry companies employ another 100,000 workers. Many of these are centred in the [Yvelines](/wiki/Yvelines "Yvelines") department, around the Renault and PSA-Citroën plants. This department alone employs 33,000.^[\[194\]](#cite_note-INSEE_201210-195)^ In 2014, the industry as a whole suffered a major loss, with the closing of a major [Aulnay-sous-Bois](/wiki/Aulnay-sous-Bois "Aulnay-sous-Bois") Citroën assembly plant.^[\[194\]](#cite_note-INSEE_201210-195)^{#cite_ref-INSEE_201210_195-8}

The southern [Essonne](/wiki/Essonne "Essonne") department specialises in science and technology.^[\[194\]](#cite_note-INSEE_201210-195)^ The south-eastern [Val-de-Marne](/wiki/Val-de-Marne "Val-de-Marne"), with its wholesale [Rungis food market](/wiki/March%C3%A9_international_de_Rungis "Marché international de Rungis"), specialises in food processing and beverages.^[\[194\]](#cite_note-INSEE_201210-195)^ The Paris region's manufacturing decline is quickly being replaced by eco-industries. These employ about 100,000 workers.^[\[194\]](#cite_note-INSEE_201210-195)^{#cite_ref-INSEE_201210_195-11}

Incomes are higher in the Western part of Paris and in the western suburbs, than in the northern and eastern parts of the urban area.^[\[199\]](#cite_note-wealth_distribution-200)^ While Paris has some of the richest neighbourhoods in France, it also has some of the poorest, mostly on the eastern side of the city. In 2012, 14 percent of households in Paris earned less than €977 per month, the official [poverty line](/wiki/Poverty_line "Poverty line"). Twenty-five percent of residents in the 19th arrondissement lived below the poverty line. In Paris' wealthiest neighbourhood, the 7th arrondissement, 7 percent lived below the poverty line.^[\[200\]](#cite_note-201)^ The unemployment rate in Paris in the 4th trimester of 2021 was six percent, compared with 7.4 percent in the whole of France. This was the lowest rate in thirteen years.^[\[201\]](#cite_note-202)^^[\[202\]](#cite_note-203)^{#cite_ref-203}  

### Tourism {#Tourism}

Main article: [Tourism in Paris](/wiki/Tourism_in_Paris "Tourism in Paris")  
Further information: [Landmarks in Paris](/wiki/Landmarks_in_Paris "Landmarks in Paris"), [Historical quarters of Paris](/wiki/Historical_quarters_of_Paris "Historical quarters of Paris"), and [List of tourist attractions in Paris](/wiki/List_of_tourist_attractions_in_Paris "List of tourist attractions in Paris")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Visiter_le_Louvre_en_%C3%A9t%C3%A9_%21_%284787187477%29.jpg/170px-Visiter_le_Louvre_en_%C3%A9t%C3%A9_%21_%284787187477%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Visiter_le_Louvre_en_%C3%A9t%C3%A9_!_(4787187477).jpg) The [Louvre](/wiki/Louvre "Louvre"), the [most-visited art museum in the world](/wiki/List_of_most-visited_art_museums "List of most-visited art museums")

Tourism continued to recover in the Paris region in 2022, increasing to 44 million visitors, an increase of 95 percent over 2021, but still 13 percent lower than in 2019.^[\[203\]](#cite_note-pro.visitparisregion.com-204)^{#cite_ref-pro.visitparisregion.com_204-0}

[Greater Paris](/wiki/Grand_Paris "Grand Paris"), comprising Paris and its three surrounding departments, received a record 38 million visitors in 2019, measured by hotel arrivals.^[\[204\]](#cite_note-KeyFigures-205)^ These included 12.2 million French visitors. Of the foreign visitors, the greatest number came from the United States (2.6 million), United Kingdom (1.2 million), Germany (981 thousand) and China (711 thousand).^[\[204\]](#cite_note-KeyFigures-205)^{#cite_ref-KeyFigures_205-1}

In 2018, measured by the [Euromonitor](/wiki/Euromonitor "Euromonitor") Global Cities Destination Index, Paris was the second-busiest airline destination in the world, with 19.10 million visitors, behind Bangkok (22.78 million) but ahead of London (19.09 million).^[\[205\]](#cite_note-206)^ In 2016, 393,008 workers in Greater Paris, or 12.4 percent of the total workforce, were engaged in tourism-related sectors such as hotels, catering, transport and leisure.^[\[206\]](#cite_note-TIP2016-207)^{#cite_ref-TIP2016_207-0}

Paris' top cultural attractions in 2022 were the [Louvre Museum](/wiki/Louvre_Museum "Louvre Museum") (7.7 million visitors), the [Eiffel Tower](/wiki/Eiffel_Tower "Eiffel Tower") (5.8 million visitors), the [Musée d'Orsay](/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_d%27Orsay "Musée d'Orsay") (3.27 million visitors) and the [Centre Pompidou](/wiki/Centre_Pompidou "Centre Pompidou") (3 million visitors).^[\[203\]](#cite_note-pro.visitparisregion.com-204)^{#cite_ref-pro.visitparisregion.com_204-1}

In 2019, Greater Paris had 2,056 hotels, including 94 five-star hotels, with a total of 121,646 rooms.^[\[204\]](#cite_note-KeyFigures-205)^ In 2019, in addition to the hotels, Greater Paris had 60,000 homes registered with [Airbnb](/wiki/Airbnb "Airbnb").^[\[204\]](#cite_note-KeyFigures-205)^ Under French law, renters of these units must pay the Paris tourism tax. The company paid the city government 7.3 million euros in 2016.^[\[207\]](#cite_note-208)^^[\[208\]](#cite_note-209)^^\[*[full citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include "Wikipedia:Citing sources")*\]^{#cite_ref-209}

A minuscule fraction of foreign visitors suffer from [Paris syndrome](/wiki/Paris_syndrome "Paris syndrome"), when their experiences do not meet expectations.^[\[209\]](#cite_note-210)^{#cite_ref-210}  

Culture {#Culture}
------------------

### Painting and sculpture {#Painting_and_sculpture}

Main article: [Art in Paris](/wiki/Art_in_Paris "Art in Paris")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Pierre-Auguste_Renoir%2C_Le_Moulin_de_la_Galette.jpg/220px-Pierre-Auguste_Renoir%2C_Le_Moulin_de_la_Galette.jpg)](/wiki/File:Pierre-Auguste_Renoir,_Le_Moulin_de_la_Galette.jpg) [Auguste Renoir](/wiki/Auguste_Renoir "Auguste Renoir"), *[Bal du moulin de la Galette](/wiki/Bal_du_moulin_de_la_Galette "Bal du moulin de la Galette")* , 1876, oil on canvas, 131 cm × 175 cm (52 in × 69 in), [Musée d'Orsay](/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_d%27Orsay "Musée d'Orsay")

For centuries, Paris has attracted artists from around the world. As a result, Paris has acquired a reputation as the "City of Art".^[\[210\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMontclos2003-211)^ Italian artists were a profound influence on the development of art in Paris in the 16th and 17th centuries, particularly in sculpture and reliefs. Painting and sculpture became the pride of the French monarchy and the French royal family commissioned many Parisian artists to adorn their palaces during the [French Baroque and Classicism](/wiki/French_Baroque_and_Classicism "French Baroque and Classicism") era. Sculptors such as [Girardon](/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Girardon "François Girardon"), [Coysevox](/wiki/Antoine_Coysevox "Antoine Coysevox") and [Coustou](/wiki/Nicolas_Coustou "Nicolas Coustou") acquired reputations as the finest artists in the royal court in 17th-century France. [Pierre Mignard](/wiki/Pierre_Mignard "Pierre Mignard") became the first painter to King [Louis XIV](/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France "Louis XIV of France") during this period. In 1648, the *[Académie royale de peinture et de sculpture](/wiki/Acad%C3%A9mie_royale_de_peinture_et_de_sculpture "Académie royale de peinture et de sculpture")* (Royal Academy of Painting and Sculpture) was established to accommodate for the dramatic interest in art in the capital. This served as France's top art school until 1793.^[\[211\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichelin2011-212)^{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichelin2011_212-0}

Paris was in its artistic prime in the 19th century and early 20th century, when it had a colony of artists established in the city and in art schools associated with some of the finest painters of the times: [Henri de Toulouse-Lautrec](/wiki/Henri_de_Toulouse-Lautrec "Henri de Toulouse-Lautrec"), [Édouard Manet](/wiki/%C3%89douard_Manet "Édouard Manet"), [Claude Monet](/wiki/Claude_Monet "Claude Monet"), [Berthe Morisot](/wiki/Berthe_Morisot "Berthe Morisot"), [Paul Gauguin](/wiki/Paul_Gauguin "Paul Gauguin"), [Pierre-Auguste Renoir](/wiki/Pierre-Auguste_Renoir "Pierre-Auguste Renoir") and others. Paris was central to the development of [Romanticism](/wiki/Romanticism "Romanticism") in art, with painters such as [Géricault](/wiki/Th%C3%A9odore_G%C3%A9ricault "Théodore Géricault").^[\[211\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichelin2011-212)^ [Impressionism](/wiki/Impressionism "Impressionism"), [Art Nouveau](/wiki/Art_Nouveau "Art Nouveau"), [Symbolism](/wiki/Symbolism_(arts) "Symbolism (arts)"), [Fauvism](/wiki/Fauvism "Fauvism"), [Cubism](/wiki/Cubism "Cubism") and [Art Deco](/wiki/Art_Deco "Art Deco") movements all evolved in Paris.^[\[211\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichelin2011-212)^ In the late 19th century, many artists in the French provinces and worldwide flocked to Paris to exhibit their works in the numerous salons and expositions and make a name for themselves.^[\[212\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerry199519-213)^ Artists such as [Pablo Picasso](/wiki/Pablo_Picasso "Pablo Picasso"), [Henri Matisse](/wiki/Henri_Matisse "Henri Matisse"), [Vincent van Gogh](/wiki/Vincent_van_Gogh "Vincent van Gogh"), [Paul Cézanne](/wiki/Paul_C%C3%A9zanne "Paul Cézanne"), [Jean Metzinger](/wiki/Jean_Metzinger "Jean Metzinger"), [Albert Gleizes](/wiki/Albert_Gleizes "Albert Gleizes"), [Henri Rousseau](/wiki/Henri_Rousseau "Henri Rousseau"), [Marc Chagall](/wiki/Marc_Chagall "Marc Chagall"), [Amedeo Modigliani](/wiki/Amedeo_Modigliani "Amedeo Modigliani") and many others became associated with Paris.{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerry199519_213-0}

The most prestigious sculptors who made their reputation in Paris in the modern era are [Frédéric Auguste Bartholdi](/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Auguste_Bartholdi "Frédéric Auguste Bartholdi") ([Statue of Liberty](/wiki/Statue_of_Liberty "Statue of Liberty")), [Auguste Rodin](/wiki/Auguste_Rodin "Auguste Rodin"), [Camille Claudel](/wiki/Camille_Claudel "Camille Claudel"), [Antoine Bourdelle](/wiki/Antoine_Bourdelle "Antoine Bourdelle"), [Paul Landowski](/wiki/Paul_Landowski "Paul Landowski") (statue of [*Christ the Redeemer*](/wiki/Christ_the_Redeemer_(statue) "Christ the Redeemer (statue)") in [Rio de Janeiro](/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro "Rio de Janeiro")) and [Aristide Maillol](/wiki/Aristide_Maillol "Aristide Maillol"). The [Golden Age](/wiki/Belle_%C3%89poque "Belle Époque") of the [School of Paris](/wiki/School_of_Paris "School of Paris") ended between the two world wars.  

### Museums {#Museums}

Main article: [List of museums in Paris](/wiki/List_of_museums_in_Paris "List of museums in Paris")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/MuseeOrsay_20070324.jpg/220px-MuseeOrsay_20070324.jpg)](/wiki/File:MuseeOrsay_20070324.jpg) [Musée d'Orsay](/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_d%27Orsay "Musée d'Orsay")

The [Louvre](/wiki/Louvre "Louvre") received 2,8 million visitors in 2021, up from 2.7 million in 2020,^[\[213\]](#cite_note-ReferenceC-214)^ holding its position as first among the [most-visited museums](/wiki/List_of_most-visited_museums "List of most-visited museums"). Its treasures include the *[Mona Lisa](/wiki/Mona_Lisa "Mona Lisa")* (*La Joconde* ), the *[Venus de Milo](/wiki/Venus_de_Milo "Venus de Milo")* statue, and *[Liberty Leading the People](/wiki/Liberty_Leading_the_People "Liberty Leading the People")* . The second-most visited museum in the city in 2021, with 1.5 million visitors, was the [Centre Georges Pompidou](/wiki/Centre_Georges_Pompidou "Centre Georges Pompidou"), also known as Beaubourg, which houses the [Musée National d'Art Moderne](/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_National_d%27Art_Moderne "Musée National d'Art Moderne") The third most visited Paris museum in 2021 was the [National Museum of Natural History](/wiki/National_Museum_of_Natural_History,_France "National Museum of Natural History, France") with 1,4 million visitors. It is famous for its dinosaur artefacts, mineral collections and its Gallery of Evolution. It was followed by the [Musée d'Orsay](/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_d%27Orsay "Musée d'Orsay"), featuring 19th century art and the [French Impressionists](/wiki/French_Impressionists "French Impressionists"), which had one million visitors. Paris hosts one of the largest science museums in Europe, the [Cité des sciences et de l'industrie](/wiki/Cit%C3%A9_des_sciences_et_de_l%27industrie "Cité des sciences et de l'industrie"), (984,000 visitors in 2020). The other most-visited Paris museums in 2021 were the [Fondation Louis Vuitton](/wiki/Fondation_Louis_Vuitton "Fondation Louis Vuitton") (691,000), the [Musée du Quai Branly -- Jacques Chirac](/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_du_Quai_Branly_%E2%80%93_Jacques_Chirac "Musée du Quai Branly – Jacques Chirac"), featuring the indigenous art and cultures of Africa, Asia, Oceania, and the Americas. (616,000); the [Musée Carnavalet](/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_Carnavalet "Musée Carnavalet") (History of Paris) (606,000), and the [Petit Palais](/wiki/Petit_Palais "Petit Palais"), the art museum of the City of Paris (518,000).^[\[214\]](#cite_note-215)^{#cite_ref-215}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Mus%C3%A9e_du_quai_Branly.jpg/220px-Mus%C3%A9e_du_quai_Branly.jpg)](/wiki/File:Mus%C3%A9e_du_quai_Branly.jpg) [Musée du quai Branly](/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_du_Quai_Branly_%E2%80%93_Jacques_Chirac "Musée du Quai Branly – Jacques Chirac")

The [Musée de l'Orangerie](/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_de_l%27Orangerie "Musée de l'Orangerie"), near both the Louvre and the Orsay, also exhibits Impressionists and Post-Impressionists, including most of [Claude Monet](/wiki/Claude_Monet "Claude Monet")'s large *[Water Lilies](/wiki/Water_Lilies_(Monet_series) "Water Lilies (Monet series)")* murals. The [Musée national du Moyen Âge](/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_national_du_Moyen_%C3%82ge "Musée national du Moyen Âge"), or Cluny Museum, presents [Medieval art](/wiki/Medieval_art "Medieval art"). The [Guimet Museum](/wiki/Guimet_Museum "Guimet Museum"), or *Musée national des arts asiatiques* , has one of the largest collections of Asian art in Europe. There are also notable museums devoted to individual artists, including the [Musée Picasso](/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_Picasso "Musée Picasso"), the [Musée Rodin](/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_Rodin "Musée Rodin") and the [Musée national Eugène Delacroix](/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_national_Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix "Musée national Eugène Delacroix").

The military history of France is presented by displays at the [Musée de l'Armée](/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_de_l%27Arm%C3%A9e "Musée de l'Armée") at [Les Invalides](/wiki/Les_Invalides "Les Invalides"). In addition to the national museums, run by the [Ministry of Culture](/wiki/Ministry_of_Culture_(France) "Ministry of Culture (France)"), the City of Paris operates 14 museums, including the [Carnavalet Museum](/wiki/Carnavalet_Museum "Carnavalet Museum") on the history of Paris, [Musée d'Art Moderne de la Ville de Paris](/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_d%27Art_Moderne_de_la_Ville_de_Paris "Musée d'Art Moderne de la Ville de Paris"), [Palais de Tokyo](/wiki/Palais_de_Tokyo "Palais de Tokyo"), the [House of Victor Hugo](/wiki/Maison_de_Victor_Hugo "Maison de Victor Hugo"), the [House of Balzac](/wiki/Maison_de_Balzac "Maison de Balzac") and the [Catacombs of Paris](/wiki/Catacombs_of_Paris "Catacombs of Paris").^[\[215\]](#cite_note-216)^ There are also notable private museums. The Contemporary Art museum of the [Louis Vuitton Foundation](/wiki/Louis_Vuitton_Foundation "Louis Vuitton Foundation"), designed by architect [Frank Gehry](/wiki/Frank_Gehry "Frank Gehry"), opened in October 2014 in the [Bois de Boulogne](/wiki/Bois_de_Boulogne "Bois de Boulogne").{#cite_ref-216}  

### Theatre {#Theatre}

The largest opera houses of Paris are the 19th-century Opéra Garnier (historical [Paris Opéra](/wiki/Op%C3%A9ra_National_de_Paris "Opéra National de Paris")) and modern [Opéra Bastille](/wiki/Op%C3%A9ra_Bastille "Opéra Bastille"); the former tends toward the more classic ballets and operas, and the latter provides a mixed repertoire of classic and modern.^[\[216\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawrenceGondrand2010178-217)^ In the middle of the 19th century, there were three other active and competing opera houses: the Opéra-Comique (which still exists), [Théâtre-Italien](/wiki/Th%C3%A9%C3%A2tre-Italien "Théâtre-Italien") and [Théâtre Lyrique](/wiki/Th%C3%A9%C3%A2tre_Lyrique "Théâtre Lyrique") (which in modern times changed its profile and name to [Théâtre de la Ville](/wiki/Th%C3%A9%C3%A2tre_de_la_Ville "Théâtre de la Ville")).^[\[217\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchumacher199660-218)^ [Philharmonie de Paris](/wiki/Philharmonie_de_Paris "Philharmonie de Paris"), the modern symphonic concert hall of Paris, opened in January 2015. Another musical landmark is the [Théâtre des Champs-Élysées](/wiki/Th%C3%A9%C3%A2tre_des_Champs-%C3%89lys%C3%A9es "Théâtre des Champs-Élysées"), where the first performances of Diaghilev's [Ballets Russes](/wiki/Ballets_Russes "Ballets Russes") took place in 1913.{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchumacher199660_218-0}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/CFfacadePlacedelOpera.png/220px-CFfacadePlacedelOpera.png)](/wiki/File:CFfacadePlacedelOpera.png) The [Comédie Française](/wiki/Com%C3%A9die_Fran%C3%A7aise "Comédie Française") (Salle Richelieu)

Theatre traditionally has occupied a large place in Parisian culture, and many of its most popular actors today are also stars of French television. The oldest and most famous Paris theatre is the [Comédie-Française](/wiki/Com%C3%A9die-Fran%C3%A7aise "Comédie-Française"), founded in 1680. Run by the Government of France, it performs mostly French classics at the Salle Richelieu in the [Palais-Royal](/wiki/Palais-Royal "Palais-Royal").^[\[218\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFierro19961173-219)^ Other famous theatres include the [Odéon-Théâtre de l'Europe](/wiki/Od%C3%A9on-Th%C3%A9%C3%A2tre_de_l%27Europe "Odéon-Théâtre de l'Europe"), also a state institution and theatrical landmark; the Théâtre Mogador; and the [Théâtre de la Gaîté-Montparnasse](/wiki/Th%C3%A9%C3%A2tre_de_la_Ga%C3%AEt%C3%A9-Montparnasse "Théâtre de la Gaîté-Montparnasse").^[\[219\]](#cite_note-220)^{#cite_ref-220}

The music hall and [cabaret](/wiki/Cabaret "Cabaret") are famous Paris institutions. The *[Moulin Rouge](/wiki/Moulin_Rouge "Moulin Rouge")* was opened in 1889 and became the birthplace of the dance known as the French [Cancan](/wiki/Cancan "Cancan"). It helped make famous the singers [Mistinguett](/wiki/Mistinguett "Mistinguett") and [Édith Piaf](/wiki/%C3%89dith_Piaf "Édith Piaf") and the painter [Toulouse-Lautrec](/wiki/Henri_de_Toulouse-Lautrec "Henri de Toulouse-Lautrec"), who made posters for the venue. In 1911, the dance hall [Olympia Paris](/wiki/Olympia_Paris "Olympia Paris") invented the grand staircase as a settling for its shows, competing with its great rival, the *[Folies Bergère](/wiki/Folies_Berg%C3%A8re "Folies Bergère")* . Its stars in the 1920s included the American singer and dancer [Josephine Baker](/wiki/Josephine_Baker "Josephine Baker"). Later, Olympia Paris presented [Dalida](/wiki/Dalida "Dalida"), [Edith Piaf](/wiki/Edith_Piaf "Edith Piaf"), [Marlene Dietrich](/wiki/Marlene_Dietrich "Marlene Dietrich"), [Miles Davis](/wiki/Miles_Davis "Miles Davis"), [Judy Garland](/wiki/Judy_Garland "Judy Garland") and the [Grateful Dead](/wiki/Grateful_Dead "Grateful Dead").

The [Casino de Paris](/wiki/Casino_de_Paris "Casino de Paris") presented many famous French singers, including [Mistinguett](/wiki/Mistinguett "Mistinguett"), [Maurice Chevalier](/wiki/Maurice_Chevalier "Maurice Chevalier") and [Tino Rossi](/wiki/Tino_Rossi "Tino Rossi"). Other famous Paris music halls include *[Le Lido](/wiki/Le_Lido "Le Lido")* , on the Champs-Élysées, opened in 1946; and the [Crazy Horse Saloon](/wiki/Crazy_Horse_(cabaret) "Crazy Horse (cabaret)"), featuring strip-tease, dance and magic, opened in 1951. A half dozen music halls exist today in Paris, attended mostly by visitors to the city.^[\[220\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFierro19961005–1006-221)^{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFierro19961005–1006_221-0}  

### Literature {#Literature}

Main article: [Writers in Paris](/wiki/Writers_in_Paris "Writers in Paris")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Victor_Hugo_by_%C3%89tienne_Carjat_1876_-_full.jpg/170px-Victor_Hugo_by_%C3%89tienne_Carjat_1876_-_full.jpg)](/wiki/File:Victor_Hugo_by_%C3%89tienne_Carjat_1876_-_full.jpg) [Victor Hugo](/wiki/Victor_Hugo "Victor Hugo")

The first book printed in France, *Epistolae* ("Letters"), by [Gasparinus de Bergamo](/wiki/Gasparinus_de_Bergamo "Gasparinus de Bergamo") (Gasparino da Barzizza), was published in Paris in 1470 by the press established by [Johann Heynlin](/wiki/Johann_Heynlin "Johann Heynlin"). Since then, Paris has been the centre of the French publishing industry, the home of some of the world's best-known writers and poets, and the setting for many classic works of French literature. Paris did not become the acknowledged capital of French literature until the 17th century, with authors such as [Boileau](/wiki/Nicolas_Boileau "Nicolas Boileau"), [Corneille](/wiki/Pierre_Corneille "Pierre Corneille"), [La Fontaine](/wiki/La_Fontaine "La Fontaine"), [Molière](/wiki/Moli%C3%A8re "Molière"), [Racine](/wiki/Jean_Racine "Jean Racine"), [Charles Perrault](/wiki/Charles_Perrault "Charles Perrault"),^[\[221\]](#cite_note-222)^ several coming from the provinces, as well as the foundation of the [Académie française](/wiki/Acad%C3%A9mie_fran%C3%A7aise "Académie française").^[\[222\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFierro1996488-223)^ In the 18th century, the literary life of Paris revolved around the cafés and salons; it was dominated by [Voltaire](/wiki/Voltaire "Voltaire"), [Jean-Jacques Rousseau](/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau "Jean-Jacques Rousseau"), [Pierre de Marivaux](/wiki/Pierre_de_Marivaux "Pierre de Marivaux") and [Pierre Beaumarchais](/wiki/Pierre_Beaumarchais "Pierre Beaumarchais").{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFierro1996488_223-0}

During the 19th century, Paris was the home and subject for some of France's greatest writers, including [Charles Baudelaire](/wiki/Charles_Baudelaire "Charles Baudelaire"), [Stéphane Mallarmé](/wiki/St%C3%A9phane_Mallarm%C3%A9 "Stéphane Mallarmé"), [Mérimée](/wiki/M%C3%A9rim%C3%A9e "Mérimée"), [Alfred de Musset](/wiki/Alfred_de_Musset "Alfred de Musset"), [Marcel Proust](/wiki/Marcel_Proust "Marcel Proust"), [Émile Zola](/wiki/%C3%89mile_Zola "Émile Zola"), [Alexandre Dumas](/wiki/Alexandre_Dumas "Alexandre Dumas"), [Gustave Flaubert](/wiki/Gustave_Flaubert "Gustave Flaubert"), [Guy de Maupassant](/wiki/Guy_de_Maupassant "Guy de Maupassant") and [Honoré de Balzac](/wiki/Honor%C3%A9_de_Balzac "Honoré de Balzac"). Victor Hugo's *[The Hunchback of Notre-Dame](/wiki/The_Hunchback_of_Notre-Dame "The Hunchback of Notre-Dame")* inspired the renovation of its setting, the [Notre-Dame de Paris](/wiki/Notre-Dame_de_Paris "Notre-Dame de Paris").^[\[223\]](#cite_note-224)^ Another of Victor Hugo's works, *[Les Misérables](/wiki/Les_Mis%C3%A9rables "Les Misérables")* , described the social change and political turmoil in Paris in the early 1830s.^[\[224\]](#cite_note-225)^ One of the most popular of all French writers, [Jules Verne](/wiki/Jules_Verne "Jules Verne"), worked at the Theatre Lyrique and the Paris stock exchange, while he did research for his stories at the National Library.^[\[225\]](#cite_note-226)^{#cite_ref-226}

In the 20th century, the Paris literary community was dominated by figures such as [Colette](/wiki/Colette "Colette"), [André Gide](/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Gide "André Gide"), [François Mauriac](/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Mauriac "François Mauriac"), [André Malraux](/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Malraux "André Malraux"), [Albert Camus](/wiki/Albert_Camus "Albert Camus"), and, after World War II, by [Simone de Beauvoir](/wiki/Simone_de_Beauvoir "Simone de Beauvoir") and [Jean-Paul Sartre](/wiki/Jean-Paul_Sartre "Jean-Paul Sartre"). Between the wars it was the home of many important expatriate writers, including [Ernest Hemingway](/wiki/Ernest_Hemingway "Ernest Hemingway"), [Samuel Beckett](/wiki/Samuel_Beckett "Samuel Beckett"), [Miguel Ángel Asturias](/wiki/Miguel_%C3%81ngel_Asturias "Miguel Ángel Asturias"), [Alejo Carpentier](/wiki/Alejo_Carpentier "Alejo Carpentier") and, [Arturo Uslar Pietri](/wiki/Arturo_Uslar_Pietri "Arturo Uslar Pietri"). The winner of the 2014 [Nobel Prize in Literature](/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature "Nobel Prize in Literature"), [Patrick Modiano](/wiki/Patrick_Modiano "Patrick Modiano"), based most of his literary work on the depiction of the city during World War II and the 1960s--1970s.^[\[226\]](#cite_note-227)^{#cite_ref-227}

Paris is a city of books and bookstores. In the 1970s, 80 percent of French-language publishing houses were found in Paris.^[\[227\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFierro1996840-228)^ It is also a city of small bookstores. There are about 150 bookstores in the 5th arrondissement alone, plus another 250 book stalls along the Seine. Small Paris bookstores are protected against competition from discount booksellers by French law; books, even e-books, cannot be discounted more than five percent below their publisher's cover price.^[\[228\]](#cite_note-229)^{#cite_ref-229}  

### Music {#Music}

Main articles: [Music in Paris](/wiki/Music_in_Paris "Music in Paris") and [History of music in Paris](/wiki/History_of_music_in_Paris "History of music in Paris")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Olympia_facade.jpg/170px-Olympia_facade.jpg)](/wiki/File:Olympia_facade.jpg) [Olympia](/wiki/Olympia_(Paris) "Olympia (Paris)") music hall

In the late 12th century, a school of [polyphony](/wiki/Polyphony "Polyphony") was established at Notre-Dame. Among the [Trouvères](/wiki/Trouv%C3%A8res "Trouvères") of northern France, a group of Parisian aristocrats became known for their poetry and songs. [Troubadours](/wiki/Troubadours "Troubadours"), from the south of France, were also popular. During the reign of [François I](/wiki/Francis_I_of_France "Francis I of France"), in the [Renaissance era](/wiki/Renaissance_music "Renaissance music"), the [lute](/wiki/Lute "Lute") became popular in the French court. The French royal family and courtiers "disported themselves in masques, ballets, allegorical dances, recitals, and opera and comedy", and a national musical printing house was established.^[\[211\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichelin2011-212)^ In the [Baroque-era](/wiki/Baroque_music "Baroque music"), noted composers included [Jean-Baptiste Lully](/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Lully "Jean-Baptiste Lully"), [Jean-Philippe Rameau](/wiki/Jean-Philippe_Rameau "Jean-Philippe Rameau"), and [François Couperin](/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Couperin "François Couperin").^[\[211\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichelin2011-212)^ The [*Conservatoire de Musique de Paris*](/wiki/Conservatoire_de_Paris "Conservatoire de Paris") was founded in 1795.^[\[229\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDamschroederWilliams1990157-230)^ By 1870, Paris had become an important centre for symphony, ballet and operatic music.{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDamschroederWilliams1990157_230-0}

[Romantic-era](/wiki/Romantic_music "Romantic music") composers (in Paris) include [Hector Berlioz](/wiki/Hector_Berlioz "Hector Berlioz"), [Charles Gounod](/wiki/Charles_Gounod "Charles Gounod"), [Camille Saint-Saëns](/wiki/Camille_Saint-Sa%C3%ABns "Camille Saint-Saëns"), [Léo Delibes](/wiki/L%C3%A9o_Delibes "Léo Delibes") and [Jules Massenet](/wiki/Jules_Massenet "Jules Massenet"), among others.^[\[211\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichelin2011-212)^ [Georges Bizet](/wiki/Georges_Bizet "Georges Bizet")'s *[Carmen](/wiki/Carmen "Carmen")* premiered 3 March 1875. *Carmen* has since become one of the most popular and frequently-performed operas in the classical [canon](/wiki/Western_canon "Western canon").^[\[230\]](#cite_note-McClary-231)^^[\[231\]](#cite_note-232)^ Among the [Impressionist](/wiki/Impressionism_in_music "Impressionism in music") composers who created new works for piano, orchestra, opera, chamber music and other musical forms, stand in particular, [Claude Debussy](/wiki/Claude_Debussy "Claude Debussy"), [Erik Satie](/wiki/Erik_Satie "Erik Satie") and [Maurice Ravel](/wiki/Maurice_Ravel "Maurice Ravel"). Several foreign-born composers, such as [Frédéric Chopin](/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Chopin "Frédéric Chopin"), [Franz Liszt](/wiki/Franz_Liszt "Franz Liszt"), [Jacques Offenbach](/wiki/Jacques_Offenbach "Jacques Offenbach"), [Niccolò Paganini](/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_Paganini "Niccolò Paganini"), and [Igor Stravinsky](/wiki/Igor_Stravinsky "Igor Stravinsky"), established themselves or made significant contributions both with their works and their influence in Paris.{#cite_ref-232}

[Bal-musette](/wiki/Bal-musette "Bal-musette") is a style of French music and dance that first became popular in Paris in the 1870s and 1880s; by 1880 Paris had some 150 dance halls.^[\[232\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDregni200419-233)^ Patrons danced the [bourrée](/wiki/Bourr%C3%A9e "Bourrée") to the accompaniment of the [cabrette](/wiki/Cabrette "Cabrette") (a [bellows](/wiki/Bellows "Bellows")-blown [bagpipe](/wiki/Bagpipe "Bagpipe") locally called a "musette") and often the [vielle à roue](/wiki/Vielle_%C3%A0_roue "Vielle à roue") ([hurdy-gurdy](/wiki/Hurdy-gurdy "Hurdy-gurdy")) in the cafés and bars of the city. Parisian and Italian musicians who played the [accordion](/wiki/Accordion "Accordion") adopted the style and established themselves in Auvergnat bars,^[\[233\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDregni200832-234)^ and Paris became a major centre for [jazz](/wiki/Jazz "Jazz") and still attracts jazz musicians from all around the world to its clubs and cafés.^[\[234\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMroue2006260-235)^{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMroue2006260_235-0}

Paris is the spiritual home of [gypsy jazz](/wiki/Gypsy_jazz "Gypsy jazz") in particular, and many of the Parisian jazzmen who developed in the first half of the 20th century began by playing Bal-musette in the city.^[\[233\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDregni200832-234)^ [Django Reinhardt](/wiki/Django_Reinhardt "Django Reinhardt") rose to fame in Paris, having moved to the 18th arrondissement in a caravan as a young boy, and performed with violinist [Stéphane Grappelli](/wiki/St%C3%A9phane_Grappelli "Stéphane Grappelli") and their [Quintette du Hot Club de France](/wiki/Quintette_du_Hot_Club_de_France "Quintette du Hot Club de France") in the 1930s and 1940s.^[\[235\]](#cite_note-Gjazz-236)^{#cite_ref-Gjazz_236-0}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Moulin_Rouge%2C_Paris_April_2011.jpg/220px-Moulin_Rouge%2C_Paris_April_2011.jpg)](/wiki/File:Moulin_Rouge,_Paris_April_2011.jpg) The [Moulin Rouge](/wiki/Moulin_Rouge "Moulin Rouge") has hosted many singers including Parisian [Édith Piaf](/wiki/%C3%89dith_Piaf "Édith Piaf")

Immediately after the War the [Saint-Germain-des-Pres](/wiki/Saint-Germain-des-Pres "Saint-Germain-des-Pres") quarter and the nearby Saint-Michel quarter became home to many small jazz clubs, including the Caveau des Lorientais, the Club Saint-Germain, the Rose Rouge, the Vieux-Colombier, and the most famous, [Le Tabou](/wiki/Le_Tabou "Le Tabou"). They introduced Parisians to the music of [Claude Luter](/wiki/Claude_Luter "Claude Luter"), [Boris Vian](/wiki/Boris_Vian "Boris Vian"), [Sydney Bechet](/wiki/Sydney_Bechet "Sydney Bechet"), [Mezz Mezzrow](/wiki/Mezz_Mezzrow "Mezz Mezzrow"), and [Henri Salvador](/wiki/Henri_Salvador "Henri Salvador"). Most of the clubs closed by the early 1960s, as musical tastes shifted toward rock and roll.^[\[236\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBezbakh2004872-237)^{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBezbakh2004872_237-0}

Some of the finest [manouche](/wiki/Manouche "Manouche") musicians in the world are found here playing the cafés of the city at night.^[\[235\]](#cite_note-Gjazz-236)^ Some of the more notable jazz venues include the New Morning, Le Sunset, La Chope des Puces and Bouquet du Nord.^[\[234\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMroue2006260-235)^^[\[235\]](#cite_note-Gjazz-236)^ Several yearly festivals take place in Paris, including the [Paris Jazz Festival](/wiki/Paris_Jazz_Festival "Paris Jazz Festival") and the rock festival [Rock en Seine](/wiki/Rock_en_Seine "Rock en Seine").^[\[237\]](#cite_note-238)^ The [Orchestre de Paris](/wiki/Orchestre_de_Paris "Orchestre de Paris") was established in 1967.^[\[238\]](#cite_note-andante-239)^ December 2015 was the 100th anniversary of the birth of [Edith Piaf](/wiki/Edith_Piaf "Edith Piaf")---widely regarded as France's national [chanteuse](/wiki/Chanson "Chanson"), as well as being one of France's greatest international stars.^[\[239\]](#cite_note-yahoomusicbio-240)^{#cite_ref-yahoomusicbio_240-0}

Paris has a big [hip hop](/wiki/French_hip_hop "French hip hop") scene. This music became popular during the 1980s.^[\[240\]](#cite_note-241)^ The presence of a large African and Caribbean community helped to its development, giving political and social status for many minorities.^[\[241\]](#cite_note-242)^{#cite_ref-242}  

### Cinema {#Cinema}

See also: [List of films set in Paris](/wiki/List_of_films_set_in_Paris "List of films set in Paris")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Cin%C3%A9matographe_Lumi%C3%A8re.jpg/220px-Cin%C3%A9matographe_Lumi%C3%A8re.jpg)](/wiki/File:Cin%C3%A9matographe_Lumi%C3%A8re.jpg) Poster for the [Lumière brothers](/wiki/Auguste_and_Louis_Lumi%C3%A8re "Auguste and Louis Lumière") 1895 film *[L'Arroseur Arrosé](/wiki/L%27Arroseur_Arros%C3%A9 "L'Arroseur Arrosé")*, the earliest comedy, and the first film to portray a fictional story.

The movie industry was born in Paris when [Auguste and Louis Lumière](/wiki/Auguste_and_Louis_Lumi%C3%A8re "Auguste and Louis Lumière") projected the first motion picture for a paying audience at the Grand Café on 28 December 1895.^[\[242\]](#cite_note-243)^ Many of Paris's concert/dance halls were transformed into cinemas when the media became popular beginning in the 1930s. Paris's largest cinema room today is in the [Grand Rex](/wiki/Grand_Rex "Grand Rex") theatre with 2,700 seats.^[\[243\]](#cite_note-244)^ Big multiplex cinemas have been built since the 1990s. UGC Ciné Cité Les Halles with 27 screens, MK2 Bibliothèque with 20 screens and UGC Ciné Cité Bercy with 18 screens are among the largest.^[\[244\]](#cite_note-245)^{#cite_ref-245}

Parisians tend to share the same movie-going trends as many of the world's global cities, with cinemas primarily dominated by Hollywood-generated film entertainment. [French cinema](/wiki/Cinema_of_France "Cinema of France") comes a close second, with major directors (*réalisateurs* ) such as [Claude Lelouch](/wiki/Claude_Lelouch "Claude Lelouch"), [Jean-Luc Godard](/wiki/Jean-Luc_Godard "Jean-Luc Godard"), and [Luc Besson](/wiki/Luc_Besson "Luc Besson"), and the more slapstick/popular genre with director [Claude Zidi](/wiki/Claude_Zidi "Claude Zidi") as an example. European and Asian films are also widely shown and appreciated.^[\[245\]](#cite_note-246)^{#cite_ref-246}  

### Restaurants and cuisine {#Cuisine}

See also: [French cuisine](/wiki/French_cuisine "French cuisine")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Le_Zimmer%2C_restaurant_in_Paris.jpg/220px-Le_Zimmer%2C_restaurant_in_Paris.jpg)](/wiki/File:Le_Zimmer,_restaurant_in_Paris.jpg) Le Zimmer, on the [Place du Châtelet](/wiki/Place_du_Ch%C3%A2telet "Place du Châtelet")

Since the late 18th century, Paris has been famous for its restaurants and *[haute cuisine](/wiki/Haute_cuisine "Haute cuisine")* , food meticulously prepared and artfully presented. A luxury restaurant, La Taverne Anglaise, opened in 1786 in the arcades of the [Palais-Royal](/wiki/Palais-Royal "Palais-Royal") by [Antoine Beauvilliers](/wiki/Antoine_Beauvilliers "Antoine Beauvilliers"); it became a model for future Paris restaurants. The restaurant [Le Grand Véfour](/wiki/Le_Grand_V%C3%A9four "Le Grand Véfour") in the Palais-Royal dates from the same period.^[\[246\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFierro19961136–1138-247)^ The famous Paris restaurants of the 19th century, including the Café de Paris, the [Rocher de Cancale](/wiki/Au_Rocher_de_Cancale "Au Rocher de Cancale"), the [Café Anglais](/wiki/Caf%C3%A9_Anglais "Café Anglais"), [Maison Dorée](/wiki/Maison_dor%C3%A9e_(Paris) "Maison dorée (Paris)") and the Café Riche, were mostly located near the theatres on the [Boulevard des Italiens](/wiki/Boulevard_des_Italiens "Boulevard des Italiens"). Several of the best-known restaurants in Paris today appeared during the *[Belle Époque](/wiki/Belle_%C3%89poque "Belle Époque")* , including [Maxim's](/wiki/Maxim%27s_Paris "Maxim's Paris") on Rue Royale, [Ledoyen](/wiki/Ledoyen "Ledoyen") in the gardens of the [Champs-Élysées](/wiki/Champs-%C3%89lys%C3%A9es "Champs-Élysées"), and the [Tour d'Argent](/wiki/Tour_d%27Argent "Tour d'Argent") on the Quai de la Tournelle.^[\[247\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFierro19961137-248)^{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFierro19961137_248-0}

Today, owing to Paris's cosmopolitan population, every French regional cuisine and almost every national cuisine in the world can be found there; the city has more than 9,000 restaurants.^[\[248\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDominé2014-249)^ The [Michelin Guide](/wiki/Michelin_Guide "Michelin Guide") has been a standard guide to French restaurants since 1900, awarding its highest award, three stars, to the best restaurants in France. In 2018, of the 27 Michelin three-star restaurants in France, ten are located in Paris. These include both restaurants which serve classical French cuisine, such as [L'Ambroisie](/wiki/L%27Ambroisie "L'Ambroisie") in the Place des Vosges, and those which serve non-traditional menus, such as [L'Astrance](/wiki/L%27Astrance "L'Astrance"), which combines French and Asian cuisines. Several of France's most famous chefs, including [Pierre Gagnaire](/wiki/Pierre_Gagnaire "Pierre Gagnaire"), [Alain Ducasse](/wiki/Alain_Ducasse "Alain Ducasse"), [Yannick Alléno](/wiki/Yannick_All%C3%A9no "Yannick Alléno") and [Alain Passard](/wiki/Alain_Passard "Alain Passard"), have three-star restaurants in Paris.^[\[249\]](#cite_note-250)^^[\[250\]](#cite_note-251)^{#cite_ref-251}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Lesdeuxmagots.jpg/220px-Lesdeuxmagots.jpg)](/wiki/File:Lesdeuxmagots.jpg) [Les Deux Magots](/wiki/Les_Deux_Magots "Les Deux Magots") café on Boulevard Saint-Germain

Paris has several other kinds of traditional eating places. The [café](/wiki/Coffeehouse "Coffeehouse") arrived in Paris in the 17th century, and by the 18th century Parisian cafés were centres of the city's political and cultural life. The [Café Procope](/wiki/Caf%C3%A9_Procope "Café Procope") on the Left Bank dates from this period. In the 20th century, the cafés of the Left Bank, especially [Café de la Rotonde](/wiki/Caf%C3%A9_de_la_Rotonde "Café de la Rotonde") and [Le Dôme Café](/wiki/Le_D%C3%B4me_Caf%C3%A9 "Le Dôme Café") in Montparnasse and [Café de Flore](/wiki/Caf%C3%A9_de_Flore "Café de Flore") and [Les Deux Magots](/wiki/Les_Deux_Magots "Les Deux Magots") on Boulevard Saint Germain, all still in business, were important meeting places for painters, writers and philosophers.^[\[247\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFierro19961137-248)^ A [bistro](/wiki/Bistro "Bistro") is a type of eating place loosely defined as a neighbourhood restaurant with a modest decor and prices and a regular clientele and a congenial atmosphere. Real bistros are increasingly rare in Paris, due to rising costs, competition, and different eating habits of Parisian diners.^[\[251\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFierro1996715-252)^ A [brasserie](/wiki/Brasserie "Brasserie") originally was a tavern located next to a brewery, which served beer and food at any hour. Beginning with the [Paris Exposition of 1867](/wiki/Paris_Exposition_of_1867 "Paris Exposition of 1867"), it became a popular kind of restaurant which featured beer and other beverages served by young women in the national costume associated with the beverage. Now brasseries, like cafés, serve food and drinks throughout the day.^[\[252\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFierro1996773-253)^{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFierro1996773_253-0}  

### Fashion {#Fashion}

Main article: [Fashion in Paris](/wiki/Fashion_in_Paris "Fashion in Paris")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Magdalena_Frackowiak.jpg/170px-Magdalena_Frackowiak.jpg)](/wiki/File:Magdalena_Frackowiak.jpg) [Magdalena Frackowiak](/wiki/Magdalena_Frackowiak "Magdalena Frackowiak") at [Paris Fashion Week](/wiki/Paris_Fashion_Week "Paris Fashion Week") (Autumn 2011)

Since the 19th century, Paris has been an international [fashion capital](/wiki/Fashion_capital "Fashion capital"), particularly in the domain of [haute couture](/wiki/Haute_couture "Haute couture") (clothing hand-made to order for private clients).^[\[253\]](#cite_note-254)^ It is home to some of the largest fashion houses in the world, including [Dior](/wiki/Christian_Dior_S.A. "Christian Dior S.A.") and [Chanel](/wiki/Chanel "Chanel"), as well as many other well-known and more contemporary fashion designers, such as [Karl Lagerfeld](/wiki/Karl_Lagerfeld "Karl Lagerfeld"), [Jean-Paul Gaultier](/wiki/Jean-Paul_Gaultier "Jean-Paul Gaultier"), [Yves Saint Laurent](/wiki/Yves_Saint_Laurent_(designer) "Yves Saint Laurent (designer)"), [Givenchy](/wiki/Givenchy "Givenchy"), and [Christian Lacroix](/wiki/Christian_Lacroix "Christian Lacroix"). [Paris Fashion Week](/wiki/Paris_Fashion_Week "Paris Fashion Week"), held in January and July in the [Carrousel du Louvre](/wiki/Carrousel_du_Louvre "Carrousel du Louvre") among other renowned city locations, is one of the top four events on the international fashion calendar.^[\[254\]](#cite_note-255)^^[\[255\]](#cite_note-256)^ Moreover, Paris is also the home of the world's largest [cosmetics](/wiki/Cosmetics "Cosmetics") company: [L'Oréal](/wiki/L%27Or%C3%A9al "L'Oréal") as well as three of the top five global makers of luxury fashion accessories: [Louis Vuitton](/wiki/Louis_Vuitton "Louis Vuitton"), [Hermés](/wiki/Herm%C3%A9s "Hermés"), and [Cartier](/wiki/Cartier_(jeweler) "Cartier (jeweler)").^[\[256\]](#cite_note-257)^ Most of the major fashion designers have their showrooms along the [Avenue Montaigne](/wiki/Avenue_Montaigne "Avenue Montaigne"), between the [Champs-Élysées](/wiki/Champs-%C3%89lys%C3%A9es "Champs-Élysées") and the Seine.{#cite_ref-257}  

### Photography {#Photography}

The inventor [Nicéphore Niépce](/wiki/Nic%C3%A9phore_Ni%C3%A9pce "Nicéphore Niépce") produced the first permanent photograph on a polished pewter plate in Paris in 1825. In 1839, after the death of Niépce, [Louis Daguerre](/wiki/Louis_Daguerre "Louis Daguerre") patented the [Daguerrotype](/wiki/Daguerrotype "Daguerrotype"), which became the most common form of photography until the 1860s.^[\[211\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichelin2011-212)^ The work of [Étienne-Jules Marey](/wiki/%C3%89tienne-Jules_Marey "Étienne-Jules Marey") in the 1880s contributed considerably to the development of modern photography. Photography came to occupy a central role in Parisian Surrealist activity, in the works of [Man Ray](/wiki/Man_Ray "Man Ray") and [Maurice Tabard](/wiki/Maurice_Tabard "Maurice Tabard").^[\[257\]](#cite_note-258)^^[\[258\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHazan2011362-259)^ Numerous photographers achieved renown for their photography of Paris, including [Eugène Atget](/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Atget "Eugène Atget"), noted for his depictions of street scenes, [Robert Doisneau](/wiki/Robert_Doisneau "Robert Doisneau"), noted for his playful pictures of people and market scenes (among which *Le baiser de l'hôtel de ville* has become iconic of the romantic vision of Paris), [Marcel Bovis](/wiki/Marcel_Bovis "Marcel Bovis"), noted for his night scenes, as well as others such as [Jacques-Henri Lartigue](/wiki/Jacques-Henri_Lartigue "Jacques-Henri Lartigue") and [Henri Cartier-Bresson](/wiki/Henri_Cartier-Bresson "Henri Cartier-Bresson").^[\[211\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichelin2011-212)^ [Poster art](/wiki/Poster_art "Poster art") also became an important art form in Paris in the late nineteenth century, through the work of [Henri de Toulouse-Lautrec](/wiki/Henri_de_Toulouse-Lautrec "Henri de Toulouse-Lautrec"), [Jules Chéret](/wiki/Jules_Ch%C3%A9ret "Jules Chéret"), [Eugène Grasset](/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Grasset "Eugène Grasset"), [Adolphe Willette](/wiki/Adolphe_Willette "Adolphe Willette"), [Pierre Bonnard](/wiki/Pierre_Bonnard "Pierre Bonnard"), [Georges de Feure](/wiki/Georges_de_Feure "Georges de Feure"), [Henri-Gabriel Ibels](/wiki/Henri-Gabriel_Ibels "Henri-Gabriel Ibels"), [Paul Gavarni](/wiki/Paul_Gavarni "Paul Gavarni") and [Alphonse Mucha](/wiki/Alphonse_Mucha "Alphonse Mucha").^[\[211\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichelin2011-212)^{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichelin2011_212-8}  

### Media {#Media}

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Si%C3%A8ge_Figaro%2C_14_boulevard_Haussmann%2C_Paris_9e.jpg/170px-Si%C3%A8ge_Figaro%2C_14_boulevard_Haussmann%2C_Paris_9e.jpg)](/wiki/File:Si%C3%A8ge_Figaro,_14_boulevard_Haussmann,_Paris_9e.jpg) Founded in 1826, *[Le Figaro](/wiki/Le_Figaro "Le Figaro")* is still considered a [newspaper of record](/wiki/Newspaper_of_record "Newspaper of record").^[\[259\]](#cite_note-260)^

Paris and its close suburbs are home to numerous newspapers, magazines and publications including *[Le Monde](/wiki/Le_Monde "Le Monde")* , *[Le Figaro](/wiki/Le_Figaro "Le Figaro")* , *[Libération](/wiki/Lib%C3%A9ration "Libération")* , *[Le Nouvel Observateur](/wiki/Le_Nouvel_Observateur "Le Nouvel Observateur")* , *[Le Canard enchaîné](/wiki/Le_Canard_encha%C3%AEn%C3%A9 "Le Canard enchaîné")* , *[La Croix](/wiki/La_Croix_(newspaper) "La Croix (newspaper)")* , *[Le Parisien](/wiki/Le_Parisien "Le Parisien")* (in *[Saint-Ouen](/wiki/Saint-Ouen,_Seine-Saint-Denis "Saint-Ouen, Seine-Saint-Denis")* ), *[Les Échos](/wiki/Les_%C3%89chos_(France) "Les Échos (France)")* , *Paris Match ([Neuilly-sur-Seine](/wiki/Neuilly-sur-Seine "Neuilly-sur-Seine"))* , *Réseaux \& Télécoms* , [Reuters](/wiki/Reuters "Reuters") France, *[l'Équipe](/wiki/L%27%C3%89quipe "L'Équipe")* ([Boulogne-Billancourt](/wiki/Boulogne-Billancourt "Boulogne-Billancourt")) and *L'Officiel des Spectacles* .^[\[260\]](#cite_note-261)^ France's two most prestigious newspapers, *Le Monde* and *Le Figaro* , are the centrepieces of the Parisian publishing industry.^[\[261\]](#cite_note-262)^ [Agence France-Presse](/wiki/Agence_France-Presse "Agence France-Presse") is France's oldest, and one of the world's oldest, continually operating news agencies. AFP, as it is colloquially abbreviated, maintains its headquarters in Paris, as it has since 1835.^[\[262\]](#cite_note-263)^ [France 24](/wiki/France_24 "France 24") is a television news channel owned and operated by the French government, and is based in Paris.^[\[263\]](#cite_note-264)^ Another news agency is France Diplomatie, owned and operated by the [Ministry of Foreign and European Affairs](/wiki/Ministry_of_Foreign_and_European_Affairs_(France) "Ministry of Foreign and European Affairs (France)"), and pertains solely to diplomatic news and occurrences.^[\[264\]](#cite_note-265)^{#cite_ref-265}

The most-viewed network in France, [TF1](/wiki/TF1 "TF1"), is in nearby [Boulogne-Billancourt](/wiki/Boulogne-Billancourt "Boulogne-Billancourt"). [France 2](/wiki/France_2 "France 2"), [France 3](/wiki/France_3 "France 3"), [Canal+](/wiki/Canal%2B_(French_TV_channel) "Canal+ (French TV channel)"), [France 5](/wiki/France_5 "France 5"), [M6](/wiki/M6_(TV_channel) "M6 (TV channel)") ([Neuilly-sur-Seine](/wiki/Neuilly-sur-Seine "Neuilly-sur-Seine")), [Arte](/wiki/Arte "Arte"), [D8](/wiki/D8_(TV_channel) "D8 (TV channel)"), [W9](/wiki/W9_(TV_channel) "W9 (TV channel)"), [NT1](/wiki/NT1_(TV_channel) "NT1 (TV channel)"), [NRJ 12](/wiki/NRJ_12 "NRJ 12"), [La Chaîne parlementaire](/wiki/La_Cha%C3%AEne_parlementaire "La Chaîne parlementaire"), [France 4](/wiki/France_4 "France 4"), [BFM TV](/wiki/BFM_TV "BFM TV"), and [Gulli](/wiki/Gulli "Gulli") are other stations located in and around the capital.^[\[265\]](#cite_note-266)^ [Radio France](/wiki/Radio_France "Radio France"), France's public radio broadcaster, and its various channels, is headquartered in Paris's [16th arrondissement](/wiki/16th_arrondissement_of_Paris "16th arrondissement of Paris"). [Radio France Internationale](/wiki/Radio_France_Internationale "Radio France Internationale"), another public broadcaster is also based in the city.^[\[266\]](#cite_note-267)^ Paris also holds the headquarters of the [La Poste](/wiki/La_Poste_(France) "La Poste (France)"), France's national postal carrier.^[\[267\]](#cite_note-268)^{#cite_ref-268}  

### Holidays and festivals {#Holidays_and_festivals}

[Bastille Day](/wiki/Bastille_Day "Bastille Day"), a celebration of the storming of the Bastille in 1789, the biggest festival in the city, is a military parade taking place every year on 14 July on the [Champs-Élysées](/wiki/Champs-%C3%89lys%C3%A9es "Champs-Élysées"), from the [Arc de Triomphe](/wiki/Arc_de_Triomphe "Arc de Triomphe") to [Place de la Concorde](/wiki/Place_de_la_Concorde "Place de la Concorde"). It includes a [flypast](/wiki/Flypast "Flypast") over the Champs Élysées by the [Patrouille de France](/wiki/Patrouille_de_France "Patrouille de France"), a parade of military units and equipment, and a display of fireworks in the evening, the most spectacular being the one at the Eiffel Tower.^[\[268\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlackmoreMcConnachie2004204-269)^{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlackmoreMcConnachie2004204_269-0}

Some other yearly festivals are [Paris-Plages](/wiki/Paris-Plages "Paris-Plages"), a festive summertime event when the Right Bank of the Seine is converted into a temporary beach;^[\[268\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlackmoreMcConnachie2004204-269)^ [Journées du Patrimoine](/wiki/European_Heritage_Days "European Heritage Days"), [Fête de la Musique](/wiki/F%C3%AAte_de_la_Musique "Fête de la Musique"), Techno Parade, [Nuit Blanche](/wiki/Nuit_Blanche "Nuit Blanche"), Cinéma au clair de lune, Printemps des rues, Festival d'automne, and Fête des jardins. The [Carnaval de Paris](/wiki/Paris_Carnival "Paris Carnival"), one of the oldest festivals in Paris, dates back to the Middle Ages.{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlackmoreMcConnachie2004204_269-1}  

### Libraries {#Libraries}

Main article: [Libraries in Paris](/wiki/Libraries_in_Paris "Libraries in Paris")

The *[Bibliothèque nationale de France](/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_nationale_de_France "Bibliothèque nationale de France")* (BnF) operates public libraries in Paris, among them the François Mitterrand Library, Richelieu Library, Louvois, Opéra Library, and [Arsenal Library](/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_de_l%27Arsenal "Bibliothèque de l'Arsenal").^[\[269\]](#cite_note-270)^{#cite_ref-270}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Salle_de_lecture_Bibliotheque_Sainte-Genevieve_n01.jpg/220px-Salle_de_lecture_Bibliotheque_Sainte-Genevieve_n01.jpg)](/wiki/File:Salle_de_lecture_Bibliotheque_Sainte-Genevieve_n01.jpg) [Sainte-Geneviève Library](/wiki/Sainte-Genevi%C3%A8ve_Library "Sainte-Geneviève Library")

The [Bibliothèque Forney](/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_Forney "Bibliothèque Forney"), in the Marais district, is dedicated to the decorative arts; the Arsenal Library occupies a former military building, and has a large collection on French literature; and the [Bibliothèque historique de la ville de Paris](/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_historique_de_la_ville_de_Paris "Bibliothèque historique de la ville de Paris"), also in Le Marais, contains the Paris historical research service. The [Sainte-Geneviève Library](/wiki/Sainte-Genevi%C3%A8ve_Library "Sainte-Geneviève Library"), designed by [Henri Labrouste](/wiki/Henri_Labrouste "Henri Labrouste") and built in the mid-1800s, contains a rare book and manuscript division.^[\[270\]](#cite_note-Woodward2006-271)^ [Bibliothèque Mazarine](/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_Mazarine "Bibliothèque Mazarine") is the oldest public library in France. The [Médiathèque Musicale Mahler](/wiki/M%C3%A9diath%C3%A8que_Musicale_Mahler "Médiathèque Musicale Mahler") opened in 1986 and contains collections related to music. The François Mitterrand Library (nicknamed *Très Grande Bibliothèque* ) was completed in 1994 to a design of [Dominique Perrault](/wiki/Dominique_Perrault "Dominique Perrault") and contains four glass towers.^[\[270\]](#cite_note-Woodward2006-271)^{#cite_ref-Woodward2006_271-1}

There are several academic libraries and archives in Paris. The [Sorbonne Library](/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_de_la_Sorbonne "Bibliothèque de la Sorbonne") is the largest university library in Paris. In addition to the [Sorbonne](/wiki/Sorbonne_(building) "Sorbonne (building)") location, there are branches in Malesherbes, Clignancourt-Championnet, Michelet-[Institut d'Art et d'Archéologie](/wiki/Institut_d%27Art_et_d%27Arch%C3%A9ologie "Institut d'Art et d'Archéologie"), Serpente-Maison de la Recherche, and Institut des Etudes Ibériques.^[\[271\]](#cite_note-272)^ Other academic libraries include Interuniversity Pharmaceutical Library, Leonardo da Vinci University Library, Paris School of Mines Library, and the René Descartes University Library.^[\[272\]](#cite_note-nd.edu-273)^{#cite_ref-nd.edu_273-0}  

### Sports {#Sports}

See also: [Football in Paris](/wiki/Football_in_Paris "Football in Paris")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Le_Paris_Saint-Germain_re%C3%A7oit_l%27AS_Monaco_au_Parc_en_Coupe_de_France_le_26_avril_2017.jpg/220px-Le_Paris_Saint-Germain_re%C3%A7oit_l%27AS_Monaco_au_Parc_en_Coupe_de_France_le_26_avril_2017.jpg)](/wiki/File:Le_Paris_Saint-Germain_re%C3%A7oit_l%27AS_Monaco_au_Parc_en_Coupe_de_France_le_26_avril_2017.jpg) [Parc des Princes](/wiki/Parc_des_Princes "Parc des Princes")

Paris's most popular sport clubs are the [association football](/wiki/Association_football "Association football") club [Paris Saint-Germain F.C.](/wiki/Paris_Saint-Germain_F.C. "Paris Saint-Germain F.C.") and the [rugby union](/wiki/Rugby_union "Rugby union") clubs [Stade Français](/wiki/Stade_Fran%C3%A7ais "Stade Français") and [Racing 92](/wiki/Racing_92 "Racing 92") (the latter based in [Nanterre](/wiki/Nanterre "Nanterre"), a western inner suburb just outside the city proper). The 80,000-seat [Stade de France](/wiki/Stade_de_France "Stade de France"), built for the [1998 FIFA World Cup](/wiki/1998_FIFA_World_Cup "1998 FIFA World Cup"), is located just north of Paris in the commune of [Saint-Denis](/wiki/Saint-Denis,_Seine-Saint-Denis "Saint-Denis, Seine-Saint-Denis").^[\[273\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawrenceGondrand2010300–301-274)^ It is used for football, rugby union and track and field athletics. It hosts the [France national football team](/wiki/France_national_football_team "France national football team") for friendlies and major tournaments qualifiers, annually hosts the [French national rugby team](/wiki/France_national_rugby_union_team "France national rugby union team")'s home matches of the [Six Nations Championship](/wiki/Six_Nations_Championship "Six Nations Championship"), and hosts several important matches of the Stade Français rugby team.^[\[273\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawrenceGondrand2010300–301-274)^ In addition to Paris Saint-Germain F.C., the city and closer suburbs have a number of other professional and amateur football clubs: [Paris FC](/wiki/Paris_FC "Paris FC"), [Red Star FC](/wiki/Red_Star_FC "Red Star FC"), [US Créteil](/wiki/US_Cr%C3%A9teil-Lusitanos "US Créteil-Lusitanos"), [RCF Paris](/wiki/Racing_Club_de_France_Football "Racing Club de France Football") and [Stade Français Football](/wiki/Stade_Fran%C3%A7ais_(association_football) "Stade Français (association football)").{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawrenceGondrand2010300–301_274-1}

Paris hosted the [1900](/wiki/1900_Summer_Olympics "1900 Summer Olympics"), [1924](/wiki/1924_Summer_Olympics "1924 Summer Olympics") and [2024 Summer Olympics](/wiki/2024_Summer_Olympics "2024 Summer Olympics"). The city also bid for the [1992](/wiki/1992_Summer_Olympics "1992 Summer Olympics"), [2008](/wiki/2008_Summer_Olympics "2008 Summer Olympics"), and [2012 Olympic Games](/wiki/2012_Summer_Olympics "2012 Summer Olympics") but lost to [Barcelona](/wiki/Barcelona "Barcelona"), [Beijing](/wiki/Beijing "Beijing"), and [London](/wiki/London "London").

The city hosted the finals of the [1938 FIFA World Cup](/wiki/1938_FIFA_World_Cup_final "1938 FIFA World Cup final"), at the [Stade Olympique de Colombes](/wiki/Stade_Olympique_Yves-du-Manoir "Stade Olympique Yves-du-Manoir"), and the [1998 FIFA World Cup](/wiki/1998_FIFA_World_Cup_final "1998 FIFA World Cup final"), [2007 Rugby World Cup](/wiki/2007_Rugby_World_Cup_Final "2007 Rugby World Cup Final") and [2023 Rugby World Cup](/wiki/2023_Rugby_World_Cup_final "2023 Rugby World Cup final"), at the Stade de France. Paris hosted as well as the finals of the [1960](/wiki/1960_European_Nations%27_Cup_final "1960 European Nations' Cup final"), [1984](/wiki/UEFA_Euro_1984_final "UEFA Euro 1984 final") (both at [Parc des Princes](/wiki/Parc_des_Princes "Parc des Princes") stadium) and [2016](/wiki/UEFA_Euro_2016_final "UEFA Euro 2016 final") UEFA European Championships.^[\[274\]](#cite_note-275)^ Three [UEFA Champions League Finals](/wiki/UEFA_Champions_League_Finals "UEFA Champions League Finals") in the current century have also been played in the Stade de France: the [2000](/wiki/2000_UEFA_Champions_League_Final "2000 UEFA Champions League Final"), [2006](/wiki/2006_UEFA_Champions_League_Final "2006 UEFA Champions League Final") and [2022](/wiki/2022_UEFA_Champions_League_Final "2022 UEFA Champions League Final").^[\[275\]](#cite_note-276)^{#cite_ref-276}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Tour_de_france_2010_-_Champs_Elys%C3%A9es_n10.jpg/220px-Tour_de_france_2010_-_Champs_Elys%C3%A9es_n10.jpg)](/wiki/File:Tour_de_france_2010_-_Champs_Elys%C3%A9es_n10.jpg) 2010 Tour de France, [Champs Élysées](/wiki/Champs_%C3%89lys%C3%A9es "Champs Élysées")

The final stage of the most famous [bicycle racing](/wiki/Road_bicycle_racing "Road bicycle racing") in the world, [Tour de France](/wiki/Tour_de_France "Tour de France"), always finishes in Paris. Since 1975, the race has [finished on the Champs-Elysées](/wiki/Champs-%C3%89lys%C3%A9es_stage_in_the_Tour_de_France "Champs-Élysées stage in the Tour de France").^[\[276\]](#cite_note-277)^ [Tennis](/wiki/Tennis "Tennis") is another popular sport in Paris and throughout France; the [French Open](/wiki/French_Open "French Open"), held every year on the red clay of the Roland Garros National Tennis Centre,^[\[277\]](#cite_note-278)^ is one of the four [Grand Slam](/wiki/Grand_Slam_(tennis) "Grand Slam (tennis)") events of the world professional tennis tour. The 17,000-seat [Bercy Arena](/wiki/AccorHotels_Arena "AccorHotels Arena") (officially named *AccorHotels Arena* and formerly known as the *Palais Omnisports de Paris-Bercy* ) is the venue for the annual [Paris Masters](/wiki/Paris_Masters "Paris Masters") [ATP Tour](/wiki/ATP_Tour "ATP Tour") tennis tournament. The Bercy Arena also hosted the [2017 IIHF World Ice Hockey Championship](/wiki/2017_IIHF_World_Championship "2017 IIHF World Championship"), together with [Cologne](/wiki/Cologne "Cologne"), Germany. The final stages of the FIBA [EuroBasket 1951](/wiki/EuroBasket_1951 "EuroBasket 1951") and [EuroBasket 1999](/wiki/EuroBasket_1999 "EuroBasket 1999") were also played in Paris, the latter at the Palais Omnisports de Paris-Bercy.{#cite_ref-278}

[Basketball](/wiki/Basketball "Basketball") teams [Levallois SCB](/wiki/Levallois_Sporting_Club_Basket "Levallois Sporting Club Basket") and [Paris Basket Racing](/wiki/Paris_Basket_Racing "Paris Basket Racing") merged in 2007 to create club [Metropolitans 92](/wiki/Metropolitans_92 "Metropolitans 92"), which plays some of its games at the 4,000 capacity [Stade Pierre de Coubertin](/wiki/Stade_Pierre_de_Coubertin_(Paris) "Stade Pierre de Coubertin (Paris)").^[\[278\]](#cite_note-279)^ Another top-level professional team, [Nanterre 92](/wiki/Nanterre_92 "Nanterre 92"), plays in Nanterre. Founded in 2018, [Paris Basketball](/wiki/Paris_Basketball "Paris Basketball") has seen rapid growth to succes, winning the [2023--24 EuroCup](/wiki/2023%E2%80%9324_EuroCup_Basketball "2023–24 EuroCup Basketball").{#cite_ref-279}

Professional [Handball](/wiki/Handball "Handball") club [Paris Saint-Germain](/wiki/Paris_Saint-Germain_Handball "Paris Saint-Germain Handball") (the handball department of Paris Saint-Germain Football Club) plays in the highest tier of French handball, the [LNH Division 1](/wiki/LNH_Division_1 "LNH Division 1").

In 2023, a professional [American football](/wiki/American_football "American football") team, the [Paris Musketeers](/wiki/Paris_Musketeers "Paris Musketeers"), were formed in the city^[\[279\]](#cite_note-280)^ joining the [European League of Football](/wiki/European_League_of_Football "European League of Football").{#cite_ref-280}  

Infrastructure {#Infrastructure}
--------------------------------

### Transport {#Transport}

Main article: [Transport in Paris](/wiki/Transport_in_Paris "Transport in Paris")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Eurostar_Paris_Gare_du_Nord_Station%2C_8_October_2011.jpg/220px-Eurostar_Paris_Gare_du_Nord_Station%2C_8_October_2011.jpg)](/wiki/File:Eurostar_Paris_Gare_du_Nord_Station,_8_October_2011.jpg) The [Gare du Nord](/wiki/Gare_du_Nord "Gare du Nord") railway station is the busiest in Europe.

Paris is a major rail, highway, and air transport hub. The [Île-de-France Mobilités](/wiki/%C3%8Ele-de-France_Mobilit%C3%A9s "Île-de-France Mobilités") (IDFM) oversees the transit network in the region.^[\[280\]](#cite_note-stif-281)^ The syndicate coordinates public transport. The [RATP](/wiki/RATP_Group "RATP Group") operates 347 [bus](/wiki/Bus_(RATP) "Bus (RATP)") lines, the [Métro](/wiki/Paris_M%C3%A9tro "Paris Métro"), eight tramway lines, and sections of the RER. The [SNCF](/wiki/SNCF "SNCF") operates suburban rails, one tramway line and the other sections of the RER.
