The [Optile](/wiki/Optile "Optile") consortium of private operators manages 1,176 bus lines.^[\[281\]](#cite_note-282)^{#cite_ref-282}

Paris has one of the most [sustainable](/wiki/Sustainable_transport "Sustainable transport") transportation systems in the world,^[\[14\]](#cite_note-ICLEI-14)^^[\[282\]](#cite_note-283)^ and is one of only two cities that received the [Sustainable Transport Award](/wiki/Sustainable_Transport_Award "Sustainable Transport Award") twice, in 2008 and 2023.^[\[15\]](#cite_note-Award-15)^ In 2022--2023, 53.3% of trips in Paris were made on foot, 30% on public transport, 11.2% on bicycles and 4.3% on cars.^[\[283\]](#cite_note-284)^^[\[284\]](#cite_note-285)^ Bike lanes are being doubled, and electric car incentives are being created. Paris is banning the most polluting automobiles from key districts.^[\[285\]](#cite_note-286)^^[\[286\]](#cite_note-287)^{#cite_ref-287}  

#### Railways {#Railways}

See also: [List of Paris railway stations](/wiki/List_of_Paris_railway_stations "List of Paris railway stations")  
Main articles: [Paris Métro](/wiki/Paris_M%C3%A9tro "Paris Métro"), [Réseau Express Régional](/wiki/R%C3%A9seau_Express_R%C3%A9gional "Réseau Express Régional"), [Transilien](/wiki/Transilien "Transilien"), and [Tramways in Île-de-France](/wiki/Tramways_in_%C3%8Ele-de-France "Tramways in Île-de-France")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Paris_-_Station_de_m%C3%A9tro_Abbesses_-_PA00086748_-_001.jpg/220px-Paris_-_Station_de_m%C3%A9tro_Abbesses_-_PA00086748_-_001.jpg)](/wiki/File:Paris_-_Station_de_m%C3%A9tro_Abbesses_-_PA00086748_-_001.jpg) The [Paris Métro](/wiki/Paris_M%C3%A9tro "Paris Métro") is the busiest subway network in the European Union.

A central hub of the national rail network, Paris's six major railway stations ([Gare du Nord](/wiki/Gare_du_Nord "Gare du Nord"), [Gare de l'Est](/wiki/Gare_de_l%27Est "Gare de l'Est"), [Gare de Lyon](/wiki/Gare_de_Lyon "Gare de Lyon"), [Gare d'Austerlitz](/wiki/Gare_d%27Austerlitz "Gare d'Austerlitz"), [Gare Montparnasse](/wiki/Gare_Montparnasse "Gare Montparnasse"), [Gare Saint-Lazare](/wiki/Gare_Saint-Lazare "Gare Saint-Lazare")) and a minor one ([Gare de Bercy](/wiki/Gare_de_Bercy "Gare de Bercy")) are connected to three networks: [high-speed rail](/wiki/High-speed_rail "High-speed rail") lines ([TGV](/wiki/TGV "TGV"), [Eurostar](/wiki/Eurostar "Eurostar"), [Intercity Express](/wiki/Intercity_Express "Intercity Express"), [Frecciarossa](/wiki/Frecciarossa "Frecciarossa")), normal speed trains ([Intercités](/wiki/Intercit%C3%A9s "Intercités"), [Intercités de nuit](/wiki/Intercit%C3%A9s_de_nuit "Intercités de nuit"), [Nightjet](/wiki/Nightjet "Nightjet"), [TER](/wiki/Transport_express_r%C3%A9gional "Transport express régional")), and the suburban rails ([Transilien](/wiki/Transilien "Transilien")). The Transilien is the [commuter rail](/wiki/Commuter_rail "Commuter rail") network serving [Paris region](/wiki/%C3%8Ele-de-France "Île-de-France"), through 9 lines, 392 stations and 1,294 km (804.1 mi) of rails.

Since the inauguration of its first line in 1900, Paris's Métro network has grown to become the city's most widely used local transport system. In 2015, it carried about 5.23 million passengers daily.^[\[287\]](#cite_note-288)^ There are 16 lines, 321 stations (405 stops) and 245.6 km (152.6 mi) of rails. Superimposed on this is a "[regional express network](/wiki/R%C3%A9seau_Express_R%C3%A9gional "Réseau Express Régional")", the RER, whose five lines, 257 stops and 602 km (374 mi) of rails connect Paris to more distant parts of the urban area. With over 1.4 million passengers per day [RER A](/wiki/RER_A "RER A") is the busiest metro line in Europe. The [Paris region](/wiki/%C3%8Ele-de-France "Île-de-France") is served by [a light rail network, the tramway](/wiki/Tramways_in_%C3%8Ele-de-France "Tramways in Île-de-France"). Opened since 1992, fourteen lines are operational. The network is 186.6 kilometres (115.9 mi) long, with 278 stations.{#cite_ref-288}  

#### Air {#Air}

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/CDG_P1020431.JPG/220px-CDG_P1020431.JPG)](/wiki/File:CDG_P1020431.JPG) In 2023, [Charles de Gaulle Airport](/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle_Airport "Charles de Gaulle Airport") was the third-busiest airport in Europe and the eleventh-busiest airport in the world.^[\[288\]](#cite_note-289)^

Paris is a major international air transport hub, and the [5th busiest airport system in the world](/wiki/World%27s_busiest_city_airport_systems_by_passenger_traffic "World's busiest city airport systems by passenger traffic"). Paris is served by three commercial international airports: [Charles de Gaulle Airport](/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle_Airport "Charles de Gaulle Airport"), [Orly Airport](/wiki/Orly_Airport "Orly Airport") and [Beauvais--Tillé Airport](/wiki/Beauvais%E2%80%93Till%C3%A9_Airport "Beauvais–Tillé Airport"). In 2019, these three airports recorded traffic of 112 million passengers.^[\[289\]](#cite_note-290)^ There is also one [general aviation](/wiki/General_aviation "General aviation") airport, [Paris--Le Bourget Airport](/wiki/Paris%E2%80%93Le_Bourget_Airport "Paris–Le Bourget Airport"), historically the oldest Parisian airport and closest to the city centre, which is now used only for private business flights and air shows. Charles de Gaulle Airport, located on the edge of the northern suburbs of Paris, opened to commercial traffic in 1974 and became the busiest Parisian airport in 1993.^[\[290\]](#cite_note-291)^ In 2023, it was the [4th busiest airport in the world by international traffic](/wiki/List_of_busiest_airports_by_international_passenger_traffic "List of busiest airports by international passenger traffic") and it is the hub for the nation's [flag carrier](/wiki/Flag_carrier "Flag carrier"), [Air France](/wiki/Air_France "Air France").^[\[291\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawrenceGondrand2010278–283-292)^ Beauvais-Tillé Airport, located 69 km (43 mi) north of Paris's city centre, is used by charter airlines and [low-cost carriers](/wiki/Low-cost_carriers "Low-cost carriers").{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawrenceGondrand2010278–283_292-0}  

#### Motorways {#Motorways}

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Boulevard_P%C3%A9riph%C3%A9rique_vu_depuis_Boulevard_Auteuil_-_Paris_XVI_%28FR75%29_-_2021-08-11_-_3.jpg/220px-Boulevard_P%C3%A9riph%C3%A9rique_vu_depuis_Boulevard_Auteuil_-_Paris_XVI_%28FR75%29_-_2021-08-11_-_3.jpg)](/wiki/File:Boulevard_P%C3%A9riph%C3%A9rique_vu_depuis_Boulevard_Auteuil_-_Paris_XVI_(FR75)_-_2021-08-11_-_3.jpg) The [Boulevard Périphérique](/wiki/Boulevard_P%C3%A9riph%C3%A9rique "Boulevard Périphérique")

Paris is the most important hub of France's [motorway](/wiki/Motorway "Motorway") network, and is surrounded by three orbital freeways: the [Périphérique](/wiki/P%C3%A9riph%C3%A9rique_(Paris) "Périphérique (Paris)"),^[\[96\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawrenceGondrand201069-97)^ which follows the approximate path of 19th-century fortifications around Paris, the [A86](/wiki/A86_autoroute "A86 autoroute") motorway in the inner suburbs, and the [Francilienne](/wiki/Francilienne "Francilienne") motorway in the outer suburbs. Paris has an extensive road network with over 2,000 km (1,243 mi) of highways and motorways.{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawrenceGondrand201069_97-1}  

#### Waterways {#Waterways}

The Paris region is the most active water transport area in France. Most of the cargo is handled by the [Ports of Paris](/wiki/Autonomous_Port_of_Paris "Autonomous Port of Paris"), in facilities located around Paris. The rivers [Loire](/wiki/Loire "Loire"), [Rhine](/wiki/Rhine "Rhine"), [Rhône](/wiki/Rh%C3%B4ne "Rhône"), [Meuse](/wiki/Meuse "Meuse"), and [Scheldt](/wiki/Scheldt "Scheldt") can be reached by canals connecting with the Seine, which include the [Canal Saint-Martin](/wiki/Canal_Saint-Martin "Canal Saint-Martin"), [Canal Saint-Denis](/wiki/Canal_Saint-Denis "Canal Saint-Denis"), and the Canal de l'Ourcq.^[\[292\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJefferson2009114-293)^{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJefferson2009114_293-0}  

#### Cycling {#Cycling}

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Station_Velib_DSC_3497.JPG/220px-Station_Velib_DSC_3497.JPG)](/wiki/File:Station_Velib_DSC_3497.JPG) [Vélib'](/wiki/V%C3%A9lib%27 "Vélib'") at the [Place de la Bastille](/wiki/Place_de_la_Bastille "Place de la Bastille")

There are 440 km (270 mi) of [cycle paths and routes](/wiki/Cycling_in_Paris "Cycling in Paris") in Paris. These include *piste cyclable* , bike lanes separated from other traffic by physical barriers, and *bande cyclable* , a bicycle lane denoted by a painted path on the road). Some 29 km (18 mi) of specially marked bus lanes are free for use by cyclists, with a protective barrier against encroachments from vehicles.^[\[293\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart2004355-294)^ Cyclists have the right to ride in both directions on certain one-way streets. Paris has a [bike sharing](/wiki/Community_bicycle_program "Community bicycle program") system called [Vélib'](/wiki/V%C3%A9lib%27 "Vélib'") with more than 20,000 public bicycles distributed at 1,800 parking stations.^[\[294\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERand2010165-295)^{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTERand2010165_295-0}  

### Electricity {#Electricity}

Electricity is provided to Paris through a peripheral grid, fed by multiple sources. In 2012, around 50% of electricity generated in the [Île-de-France](/wiki/%C3%8Ele-de-France "Île-de-France") came from cogeneration energy plants. Other energy sources included thermal power (35%), waste incineration (9% -- with cogeneration plants, these provide the city in heat as well), methane gas (5%), hydraulics (1%), solar power (0.1%) and a negligible amount of wind power.^[\[295\]](#cite_note-prod_electrique_IDF-296)^ A quarter of the city's [district heating](/wiki/District_heating "District heating") is to come from a plant in [Saint-Ouen-sur-Seine](/wiki/Saint-Ouen-sur-Seine "Saint-Ouen-sur-Seine"), burning a 50/50-mix of coal and [wood pellets](/wiki/Wood_pellets "Wood pellets").^[\[296\]](#cite_note-297)^{#cite_ref-297}  

### Water and sanitation {#Water_and_sanitation}

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Ile_de_la_Cite_from_Pont_de_la_Tournelle.jpg/220px-Ile_de_la_Cite_from_Pont_de_la_Tournelle.jpg)](/wiki/File:Ile_de_la_Cite_from_Pont_de_la_Tournelle.jpg) The Seine, the [Île de la Cité](/wiki/%C3%8Ele_de_la_Cit%C3%A9 "Île de la Cité") and a [Bateau Mouche](/wiki/Bateau_Mouche "Bateau Mouche")

Paris in its early history had only the rivers Seine and [Bièvre](/wiki/Bi%C3%A8vre_(river) "Bièvre (river)") for water. From 1809, the [Canal de l'Ourcq](/wiki/Canal_de_l%27Ourcq "Canal de l'Ourcq") provided Paris with water from less-polluted rivers to the north-east of the capital.^[\[297\]](#cite_note-298)^ From 1857, the civil engineer [Eugène Belgrand](/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Belgrand "Eugène Belgrand"), under [Napoleon III](/wiki/Napoleon_III "Napoleon III"), oversaw the construction of a series of new aqueducts that brought water from locations all around the city to several reservoirs.^[\[298\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurchell197193-299)^{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurchell197193_299-0}

From then on, the new reservoir system became Paris's principal source of drinking water. The remains of the old system, pumped into lower levels of the same reservoirs, were from then on used for the cleaning of Paris's streets. This system is still a major part of Paris's water-supply network. Today Paris has more than 2,400 km (1,491 mi) of underground sewers.^[\[299\]](#cite_note-sewers-300)^{#cite_ref-sewers_300-0}

[Air pollution](/wiki/Air_pollution "Air pollution") in Paris, from the point of view of [particulate matter](/wiki/Particulates "Particulates") (PM10), is the highest in France with 38 μg/m^3^.^[\[300\]](#cite_note-301)^ From the point of view of nitrogen dioxide pollution, Paris has one of the highest levels in the EU.^[\[301\]](#cite_note-302)^{#cite_ref-302}  

### Parks and gardens {#Parks_and_gardens}

Main articles: [List of parks and gardens in Paris](/wiki/List_of_parks_and_gardens_in_Paris "List of parks and gardens in Paris") and [History of Parks and Gardens of Paris](/wiki/History_of_Parks_and_Gardens_of_Paris "History of Parks and Gardens of Paris")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Parc_des_Buttes-Chaumont%2C_22_April_2007.jpg/220px-Parc_des_Buttes-Chaumont%2C_22_April_2007.jpg)](/wiki/File:Parc_des_Buttes-Chaumont,_22_April_2007.jpg) The lawns of the [Parc des Buttes-Chaumont](/wiki/Parc_des_Buttes-Chaumont "Parc des Buttes-Chaumont") on a sunny day

Paris has more than 421 municipal parks and gardens, covering more than 3,000 hectares and containing more than 250,000 trees.^[\[302\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJarrassé20076-303)^ Two of Paris's oldest and most famous gardens are the [Tuileries Garden](/wiki/Tuileries_Garden "Tuileries Garden"), created in 1564 for the [Tuileries Palace](/wiki/Tuileries_Palace "Tuileries Palace") and redone by [André Le Nôtre](/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Le_N%C3%B4tre "André Le Nôtre") between 1664 and 1672,^[\[303\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawrenceGondrand2010125-304)^ and the [Luxembourg Garden](/wiki/Luxembourg_Garden "Luxembourg Garden"), for the [Luxembourg Palace](/wiki/Luxembourg_Palace "Luxembourg Palace"), built for [Marie de' Medici](/wiki/Marie_de%27_Medici "Marie de' Medici") in 1612, which today houses the [Senate](/wiki/Senate_(France) "Senate (France)").^[\[304\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawrenceGondrand2010208-305)^ The *[Jardin des plantes](/wiki/Jardin_des_plantes "Jardin des plantes")* was the first botanical garden in Paris, created in 1626.^[\[305\]](#cite_note-306)^{#cite_ref-306}

Between 1853 and 1870, Emperor [Napoleon III](/wiki/Napoleon_III "Napoleon III") and the city's first director of parks and gardens, [Jean-Charles Adolphe Alphand](/wiki/Jean-Charles_Adolphe_Alphand "Jean-Charles Adolphe Alphand"), created the [Bois de Boulogne](/wiki/Bois_de_Boulogne "Bois de Boulogne"), [Bois de Vincennes](/wiki/Bois_de_Vincennes "Bois de Vincennes"), [Parc Montsouris](/wiki/Parc_Montsouris "Parc Montsouris") and [Parc des Buttes-Chaumont](/wiki/Parc_des_Buttes-Chaumont "Parc des Buttes-Chaumont"), located at the four compass points around Paris, as well as many smaller parks, squares and gardens in the Paris's quarters.^[\[306\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJarrassé2007122–161-307)^ Since 1977, the city has created 166 new parks, most notably the [Parc de la Villette](/wiki/Parc_de_la_Villette "Parc de la Villette") (1987), [Parc André Citroën](/wiki/Parc_Andr%C3%A9_Citro%C3%ABn "Parc André Citroën") (1992), [Parc de Bercy](/wiki/Parc_de_Bercy "Parc de Bercy") (1997) and [Parc Clichy-Batignolles](/wiki/Parc_Clichy-Batignolles_%E2%80%93_Martin-Luther-King "Parc Clichy-Batignolles – Martin-Luther-King") (2007).^[\[307\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJarrassé2007242–256-308)^ One of the newest parks, the [Promenade des Berges de la Seine](/wiki/Promenade_des_Berges_de_la_Seine "Promenade des Berges de la Seine") (2013), built on a former highway on the [left bank](/wiki/Rive_Gauche "Rive Gauche") of the Seine between the [Pont de l'Alma](/wiki/Pont_de_l%27Alma "Pont de l'Alma") and the [Musée d'Orsay](/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_d%27Orsay "Musée d'Orsay"), has floating gardens.{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJarrassé2007242–256_308-0}  

### Cemeteries {#Cemeteries}

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Catacombes_De_Paris.jpg/220px-Catacombes_De_Paris.jpg)](/wiki/File:Catacombes_De_Paris.jpg) The [Paris Catacombs](/wiki/Catacombs_of_Paris "Catacombs of Paris") hold the remains of approximately 6 million people.

During the Roman era, Paris' main cemetery was located on the outskirts of the [left bank](/wiki/Rive_Gauche "Rive Gauche") settlement. This changed with the rise of Catholic Christianity, where most every inner-city church had adjoining burial grounds for use by their parishes. With Paris's growth, many of these, particularly the city's largest cemetery, the [Holy Innocents' Cemetery](/wiki/Holy_Innocents%27_Cemetery "Holy Innocents' Cemetery"), were filled to overflowing. When inner-city burials were condemned from 1786, the contents of all Paris' parish cemeteries were transferred to a renovated section of [Paris's stone mines](/wiki/Mines_of_Paris "Mines of Paris"), today [place Denfert-Rochereau](/wiki/Place_Denfert-Rochereau "Place Denfert-Rochereau") in the 14th arrondissement.^[\[308\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhaley2012101-309)^^[\[309\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroadwell200792-310)^{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroadwell200792_310-0}

After a tentative creation of several smaller suburban cemeteries, the Prefect Nicholas Frochot under [Napoleon](/wiki/Napoleon "Napoleon") Bonaparte provided a more definitive solution, in the creation of three massive Parisian cemeteries outside the city limits.^[\[310\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAyers2004271-311)^ Open from 1804, these were the cemeteries of [Père Lachaise](/wiki/P%C3%A8re_Lachaise_Cemetery "Père Lachaise Cemetery"), [Montmartre](/wiki/Montmartre_Cemetery "Montmartre Cemetery"), [Montparnasse](/wiki/Montparnasse_Cemetery "Montparnasse Cemetery"), and later [Passy](/wiki/Passy_Cemetery "Passy Cemetery"). New suburban cemeteries were created in the early 20th century: The largest of these are the [Cimetière parisien de Saint-Ouen](/wiki/Saint-Ouen_Cemetery "Saint-Ouen Cemetery"), the [Cimetière parisien de Pantin](/wiki/Cimeti%C3%A8re_parisien_de_Pantin "Cimetière parisien de Pantin"), also known as Cimetière parisien de [Pantin](/wiki/Pantin "Pantin")-[Bobigny](/wiki/Bobigny "Bobigny"), the Cimetière parisien d'[Ivry](/wiki/Ivry-sur-Seine "Ivry-sur-Seine"), and the Cimetière parisien de [Bagneux](/wiki/Bagneux,_Hauts-de-Seine "Bagneux, Hauts-de-Seine").^[\[311\]](#cite_note-Parisian_Cemeteries-312)^ Famous people buried in Parisian cemeteries include [Oscar Wilde](/wiki/Oscar_Wilde "Oscar Wilde"), [Frédéric Chopin](/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Chopin "Frédéric Chopin"), [Jim Morrison](/wiki/Jim_Morrison "Jim Morrison"), [Édith Piaf](/wiki/%C3%89dith_Piaf "Édith Piaf") and [Serge Gainsbourg](/wiki/Serge_Gainsbourg "Serge Gainsbourg").^[\[312\]](#cite_note-313)^{#cite_ref-313}  

### Education {#Education}

Main article: [Education in Paris](/wiki/Education_in_Paris "Education in Paris")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Chapelle_Sainte-Ursule_de_la_Sorbonne%2C_Paris_001.jpg/170px-Chapelle_Sainte-Ursule_de_la_Sorbonne%2C_Paris_001.jpg)](/wiki/File:Chapelle_Sainte-Ursule_de_la_Sorbonne,_Paris_001.jpg) The [Sorbonne University](/wiki/Sorbonne_University "Sorbonne University")

Paris is the département with the highest proportion of highly educated people. In 2009, around 40 percent of Parisians held a *[licence](/wiki/Licence_(France) "Licence (France)")* -level diploma or higher, the highest proportion in France.^[\[313\]](#cite_note-314)^ 13 percent have no diploma, the third-lowest percentage in France. Education in Paris and the Île-de-France region employs approximately 330,000 people, 170,000 of whom are teachers and professors, teaching approximately 2.9 million students in around 9,000 primary, secondary, and higher education schools and institutions.^[\[314\]](#cite_note-idf_education-315)^{#cite_ref-idf_education_315-0}

The [University of Paris](/wiki/University_of_Paris "University of Paris"), founded in the 12th century, is often called the [Sorbonne](/wiki/University_of_Paris "University of Paris") after one of its original medieval colleges. In 1970, it was broken up into [thirteen autonomous universities](/wiki/List_of_universities_and_institutions_in_the_Paris_region "List of universities and institutions in the Paris region"), following the [student demonstrations in 1968](/wiki/May_68 "May 68"). Most of the campuses today are in the [Latin Quarter](/wiki/Latin_Quarter,_Paris "Latin Quarter, Paris") where the old university was located, while others are scattered around the city and the suburbs.^[\[315\]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECombeau2013213–214-316)^{#cite_ref-FOOTNOTECombeau2013213–214_316-0}

The Paris region hosts France's highest concentration of the *[grandes écoles](/wiki/Grandes_%C3%A9coles "Grandes écoles")* -- 55 specialised centres of higher-education outside or inside the public university structure. The prestigious public universities are usually considered *[grands établissements](/wiki/Grands_%C3%A9tablissements "Grands établissements")* . Most of the *grandes écoles* were relocated to the suburbs of Paris in the 1960s and 1970s, in new campuses much larger than the old campuses within the crowded City of Paris. The [École Normale Supérieure](/wiki/%C3%89cole_Normale_Sup%C3%A9rieure "École Normale Supérieure"), [PSL University](/wiki/Paris_Sciences_et_Lettres_University "Paris Sciences et Lettres University") has remained on rue d'Ulm in the [5th arrondissement](/wiki/5th_arrondissement_of_Paris "5th arrondissement of Paris").^[\[316\]](#cite_note-317)^{#cite_ref-317}

In 2024, Paris is the home of prestigious universities in science and technology ([Polytechnic Institute of Paris](/wiki/Polytechnic_Institute_of_Paris "Polytechnic Institute of Paris"), [Paris Cité University](/wiki/Paris_Cit%C3%A9_University "Paris Cité University"), [Paris-Saclay University](/wiki/Paris-Saclay_University "Paris-Saclay University"), [Sorbonne University](/wiki/Sorbonne_University "Sorbonne University")), political science ([Sciences Po](/wiki/Sciences_Po "Sciences Po")),^[\[317\]](#cite_note-318)^ management ([HEC Paris](/wiki/HEC_Paris "HEC Paris"), [ESSEC Business School](/wiki/ESSEC_Business_School "ESSEC Business School"), [ESCP Business School](/wiki/ESCP_Business_School "ESCP Business School"), [INSEAD](/wiki/INSEAD "INSEAD"))^[\[318\]](#cite_note-319)^ as well as multidisciplinary universities ([Paris Sciences et Lettres University](/wiki/Paris_Sciences_et_Lettres_University "Paris Sciences et Lettres University")).^[\[319\]](#cite_note-320)^{#cite_ref-320}  

### Healthcare {#Healthcare}

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/H%C3%B4tel-Dieu_de_Paris%2C_February_7%2C_2013.jpg/200px-H%C3%B4tel-Dieu_de_Paris%2C_February_7%2C_2013.jpg)](/wiki/File:H%C3%B4tel-Dieu_de_Paris,_February_7,_2013.jpg) The [Hôtel-Dieu de Paris](/wiki/H%C3%B4tel-Dieu_de_Paris "Hôtel-Dieu de Paris") is the oldest hospital in Paris.

Health care and emergency medical service in the City of Paris and its suburbs are provided by the [Assistance publique -- Hôpitaux de Paris](/wiki/Assistance_publique_%E2%80%93_H%C3%B4pitaux_de_Paris "Assistance publique – Hôpitaux de Paris") (AP-HP), a public hospital system that employs more than 90,000 people, including practitioners, support personnel, and administrators, in 44 hospitals.^[\[320\]](#cite_note-hospital-321)^ It is the largest hospital system in Europe. It provides health care, teaching, research, prevention, education and emergency medical service in 52 branches of medicine. The hospitals receive more than 5.8 million annual patient visits.^[\[320\]](#cite_note-hospital-321)^{#cite_ref-hospital_321-1}

One of the most notable hospitals is the [Hôtel-Dieu](/wiki/H%C3%B4tel-Dieu_de_Paris "Hôtel-Dieu de Paris"), founded in 651, the oldest hospital in Paris and the oldest worldwide still operating,^[\[321\]](#cite_note-322)^ although the current building is the product of a reconstruction of 1877. Other hospitals include [Pitié-Salpêtrière Hospital](/wiki/Piti%C3%A9-Salp%C3%AAtri%C3%A8re_Hospital "Pitié-Salpêtrière Hospital"), one of the largest in Europe, [Hôpital Cochin](/wiki/H%C3%B4pital_Cochin "Hôpital Cochin"), [Bichat--Claude Bernard Hospital](/wiki/Bichat%E2%80%93Claude_Bernard_Hospital "Bichat–Claude Bernard Hospital"), [Hôpital Européen Georges-Pompidou](/wiki/H%C3%B4pital_Europ%C3%A9en_Georges-Pompidou "Hôpital Européen Georges-Pompidou"), [Bicêtre Hospital](/wiki/Bic%C3%AAtre_Hospital "Bicêtre Hospital"), [Beaujon Hospital](/wiki/Beaujon_Hospital "Beaujon Hospital"), the [Curie Institute](/wiki/Curie_Institute_(Paris) "Curie Institute (Paris)"), [Lariboisière Hospital](/wiki/Lariboisi%C3%A8re_Hospital "Lariboisière Hospital"), [Necker--Enfants Malades Hospital](/wiki/Necker%E2%80%93Enfants_Malades_Hospital "Necker–Enfants Malades Hospital"), [Hôpital Saint-Louis](/wiki/H%C3%B4pital_Saint-Louis "Hôpital Saint-Louis"), [Hôpital de la Charité](/wiki/H%C3%B4pital_de_la_Charit%C3%A9 "Hôpital de la Charité") and the [American Hospital of Paris](/wiki/American_Hospital_of_Paris "American Hospital of Paris").{#cite_ref-322}  

International relations {#International_relations}
--------------------------------------------------

### International organisations {#International_organisations}

The [United Nations Educational, Scientific and Cultural Organization](/wiki/UNESCO "UNESCO") (UNESCO) has had its headquarters in Paris since November 1958. Paris is also the home of the [Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development](/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development "Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development") (OECD).^[\[322\]](#cite_note-323)^ Paris hosts the headquarters of the [European Space Agency](/wiki/European_Space_Agency "European Space Agency"), the [International Energy Agency](/wiki/International_Energy_Agency "International Energy Agency"), [European Securities and Markets Authority](/wiki/European_Securities_and_Markets_Authority "European Securities and Markets Authority") and the [European Banking Authority](/wiki/European_Banking_Authority "European Banking Authority").{#cite_ref-323}  

### Twin towns -- sister cities {#Twin_towns_.E2.80.93_sister_cities}

See also: [List of twin towns and sister cities in France](/wiki/List_of_twin_towns_and_sister_cities_in_France "List of twin towns and sister cities in France")

Since April 1956, Paris is exclusively and reciprocally [twinned](/wiki/Sister_city "Sister city") with:^[\[323\]](#cite_note-IR-324)^^[\[324\]](#cite_note-325)^{#cite_ref-325}

* [![Italy](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/03/Flag_of_Italy.svg/23px-Flag_of_Italy.svg.png)](/wiki/Italy "Italy") [Rome](/wiki/Rome "Rome"), Italy

:
    *Seule Paris est digne de Rome; seule Rome est digne de Paris.* (in French)
:
    *Solo Parigi è degna di Roma; solo Roma è degna di Parigi.* (in Italian)
:
    "Only Paris is worthy of Rome; only Rome is worthy of Paris."^[\[325\]](#cite_note-326)^^[\[326\]](#cite_note-327)^^[\[327\]](#cite_note-Paris1-328)^^[\[328\]](#cite_note-Paris2-329)^
{#cite_ref-Paris2_329-0}

Notes {#Notes}
--------------

1. **[\^](#cite_ref-24)** The word was most likely created by Parisians of the lower popular class who spoke \*argot\*, then \*parigot\* was used in a provocative manner outside the Parisian region and throughout France to mean Parisians in general.{#cite_note-24}
{#cite_note-24}
